<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>Filioque</i></h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

<p><i><b>Filioque</b></i> (<small>Ecclesiastical Latin: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Latin" title="Help:IPA for Latin">[filiˈɔkwe]</a></span>, literally "and [from] the Son"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="noprint selfreference">[<i><a href="/wiki/Talk:Filioque#Etymology" title="Talk:Filioque">discuss</a></i>]</sup>) is a Latin term added to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niceno-Constantinopolitan_Creed" title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed</a> (NCC) which is not in the original version. It has been the subject of great controversy between <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">Western Christianity</a>. The Latin term <i>Filioque</i> describes the double procession of the Holy Spirit and is translated into the English clause "and the Son" in that creed:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span style="color:gray">I believe in the Holy Spirit, the Lord, the giver of life,</span></dd>
<dd>who proceeds from the Father <strong>⟨and the Son⟩</strong>.</dd>
<dd><span style="color:gray">Who with the Father and the Son is adored and glorified.</span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Whether that term, <i>Filioque</i>, is included, and how it is translated and understood, can have important implications for how one understands the central Christian doctrine of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Trinity" title="Holy Trinity">Holy Trinity</a>. For some, the term implies a serious underestimation of <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">the Father</a>'s role in the Trinity; for others, denial of what it expresses implies a serious underestimation of the role of <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">the Son</a> in the Trinity. Over time, the term became a symbol of conflict between Eastern Christianity and Western Christianity, although there have been attempts at resolving the conflict. Among the early attempts at harmonization are the works of <a href="/wiki/Maximus_the_Confessor" title="Maximus the Confessor">Maximus the Confessor</a>, who notably was canonised independently by both Eastern and Western churches.</p>
<p>The <i>Filioque</i> is included in the form of the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> used in most Western Christian churches, first appearing in the 6th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Category:Articles_contradicting_other_articles" title="Category:Articles contradicting other articles"><span title="Other sources on the page date earliest example to 410 (November 2015)">contradictory</span></a></i>]</sup> It was accepted by the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">popes</a> only in 1014, and is rejected by the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodox_Churches" title="Oriental Orthodox Churches">Oriental Orthodox Churches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a>. It is not in the original text of this Creed, attributed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_ecumenical_council" title="Second ecumenical council">second ecumenical council</a>, Constantinople I (381), which says that the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> proceeds "from the Father", without additions of any kind, such as "and the Son" or "alone";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERCA200270_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERCA200270-3">[3]</a></sup> the Latin text now in use in most Western Churches speaks of the Holy Spirit as proceeding "from the Father <i>and the Son</i>".</p>
<p>Differences over this doctrine and the question of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primacy_of_the_Bishop_of_Rome" title="Primacy of the Bishop of Rome">papal primacy</a> have been and remain primary causes of schism between the Eastern Orthodox and Western churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELarchet2006188_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELarchet2006188-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWCCFO1979_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWCCFO1979-5">[5]</a></sup> The term has been an ongoing source of conflict between Eastern Christianity and Western Christianity, contributing, in major part, to the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a> of 1054 and proving to be an obstacle to attempts to reunify the two sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECongar195944Meyendorff1987181NAOCTC2003_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECongar195944Meyendorff1987181NAOCTC2003-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy">Controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The controversy referring to the term <i>Filioque</i> involves four separate disagreements:</p>
<ul>
<li>about the term itself</li>
<li>about the orthodoxy of the doctrine of the double procession of the Holy Spirit to which the term refers</li>
<li>about the legitimacy of inserting the term into the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr></li>
<li>about the authority of the pope to define the orthodoxy of the doctrine or to insert the term into the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr></li>
</ul>
<p>Although the disagreement about the doctrine preceded the disagreement about the insertion into the creed, the two disagreements became linked to the third when the pope approved insertion of the term into the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> in the 11th century. Siecienski writes that "[u]ltimately what was at stake was not only God's trinitarian nature, but also the nature of the Church, its teaching authority and the distribution of power among its leaders."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski20104.E2.80.935_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski20104.E2.80.935-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hubert_Cunliffe-Jones" title="Hubert Cunliffe-Jones">Hubert Cunliffe-Jones</a> identifies two opposing Eastern Orthodox opinions about the <i>Filioque</i>: a "liberal" view and a "rigorist" view. The "liberal" view sees the controversy as being largely a matter of mutual miscommunication and misunderstanding. In this view, both East and West are at fault for failing to allow for a "plurality of theologies". Each side went astray in considering their theological framework as the only one that was doctrinally valid and applicable. Thus, neither side would accept that the dispute was not so much about conflicting dogmas as it was about different <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/theologoumena" title="wikt:theologoumena">theologoumena</a></i> or theological perspectives. While all Christians must be in agreement on questions of dogma, there is room for diversity in theological approaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones2006208.E2.80.93209_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones2006208.E2.80.93209-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, this "liberal" view is vehemently opposed by those Eastern Orthodox whom Cunliffe-Jones identifies as holding a "rigorist" view. According to standard Eastern Orthodox position, as pronounced by Photius, <a href="/wiki/Mark_of_Ephesus" title="Mark of Ephesus">Mark of Ephesus</a> and 20th century Orthodox theologians such as <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lossky" title="Vladimir Lossky">Vladimir Lossky</a>, the <i>Filioque</i> question hinges on fundamental issues of dogma and cannot be dismissed as simply one of different <i>theologoumena</i>. Many in the "rigorist" camp consider the <i>Filioque</i> to have resulted in the role of the Holy Spirit being underestimated by the Western Church and thus leading to serious doctrinal error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones2006208.E2.80.93209_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones2006208.E2.80.93209-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a similar vein, Siecienski comments that, although it was common in the 20th century to view the <i>Filioque</i> as just another weapon in the power struggle between Rome and Constantinople and although this was occasionally the case, for many involved in the dispute the theological issues outweighed by far the ecclesiological concerns. According to Siecienski, the deeper question was perhaps whether Eastern and Western Christianity had wound up developing "differing and ultimately incompatible teachings about the nature of God." Moreover, Siecienski asserts that the question of whether the teachings of East and West were truly incompatible became almost secondary to the fact that, starting around the 8th or 9th century, Christians on both sides of the dispute began to believe that the differences <i>were</i> irreconcilable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski20104.E2.80.936_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski20104.E2.80.936-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the view of the West, the Eastern rejection of the <i>Filioque</i> denied the <a href="/wiki/Consubstantiality" title="Consubstantiality">consubstantiality</a> of the Father and the Son and was thus a form of crypto-<a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arianism</a>. In the East, the interpolation of the <i>Filioque</i> seemed to many to be an indication that the West was teaching a "substantially different faith". Siecienski asserts that, as much as power and authority were central issues in the debate, the strength of emotion rising even to the level of hatred can be ascribed to a belief that the other side had "destroyed the purity of the faith and refused to accept the clear teachings of the fathers on the Spirit's procession."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski20104.E2.80.936_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski20104.E2.80.936-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Filioque_controversy" title="History of the Filioque controversy">History of the Filioque controversy</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Testament">New Testament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: New Testament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=John+15:26–15:26&amp;version=nrsv" rel="nofollow">John 15:26</a> Jesus says that the Holy Spirit "But when the Helper comes, whom I will send to you from the Father, the Spirit of truth, who proceeds from the Father, he will bear witness about me".</p>
<p>In <a class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=John+16:13–15:13&amp;version=nrsv" rel="nofollow">John 16:13–15</a> Jesus says of the Holy Spirit "he will <i>take</i> what is mine and declare it to you", and it is argued that in the relations between the Persons of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> one Person cannot "take" or "receive" (<span lang="grc">λήμψεται</span>) anything from either of the others except by way of procession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Double_Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Double_Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22-10">[10]</a></sup> Texts such as <a class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=John+20:22–20:22&amp;version=nrsv" rel="nofollow">John 20:22</a> ("He breathed on them and said: Receive the Holy Spirit"), were seen by Fathers of the Church, especially <a href="/wiki/Athanasius_of_Alexandria" title="Athanasius of Alexandria">Athanasius of Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyril_of_Alexandria" title="Cyril of Alexandria">Cyril of Alexandria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epiphanius_of_Salamis" title="Epiphanius of Salamis">Epiphanius of Salamis</a> as grounds for saying that the Spirit "proceeds substantially from both" the Father and the Son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Other texts that have been used include <a class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=Galatians+4:6–4:6&amp;version=nrsv" rel="nofollow">Galatians 4:6</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=Romans+8:9–8:9&amp;version=nrsv" rel="nofollow">Romans 8:9</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=Philippians+1:19–1:19&amp;version=nrsv" rel="nofollow">Philippians 1:19</a>, where the Holy Spirit is called "the Spirit of the Son", "the Spirit of Christ", "the Spirit of Jesus Christ", and texts in the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a> on the sending of the Holy Spirit by Jesus (<a class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=John+14:16–14:16&amp;version=nrsv" rel="nofollow">14:16</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=John+15:26–15:26&amp;version=nrsv" rel="nofollow">15:26</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=John+16:7–16:7&amp;version=nrsv" rel="nofollow">16:7</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Double_Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Double_Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Siecienski asserts that "the New Testament does not explicitly address the procession of the Holy Spirit as later theology would understand the doctrine", although there are "certain principles established in the New Testament that shaped later Trinitarian theology, and particular texts that both Latins and Greeks exploited to support their respective positions vis-à-vis the <i>Filioque</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201017_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201017-12">[12]</a></sup> In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Veli-Matti_K%C3%A4rkk%C3%A4inen" title="Veli-Matti Kärkkäinen">Veli-Matti Kärkkäinen</a> says that Eastern Orthodox believe that the absence of an explicit mention of the double procession of the Holy Spirit is a strong indication that the <i>Filioque</i> is a theologically erroneous doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK.C3.A4rkk.C3.A4inen2010276_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK.C3.A4rkk.C3.A4inen2010276-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_fathers">Church fathers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Church fathers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cappadocian_Fathers">Cappadocian Fathers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Cappadocian Fathers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cappadocian_Fathers" title="Cappadocian Fathers">Cappadocian Fathers</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Basil_of_Caesarea" title="Basil of Caesarea">Basil of Caesarea</a> wrote: "Through the one Son (the Holy Spirit) is joined to the Father".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> He also said that the "natural goodness, inherent holiness, and royal dignity reaches from the Father through the only-begotten (<span lang="grc">διὰ τοῦ Μονογενοῦς</span>) to the Spirit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> However, Siecienski comments that "there are passages in Basil that are certainly capable of being read as advocating something like the <i>Filioque</i>, but to do so would be to misunderstand the inherently soteriological thrust of his work".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201040_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201040-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Nazianzus" title="Gregory of Nazianzus">Gregory of Nazianzus</a> distinguished the coming forth (<span lang="grc">προϊεον</span>) of the Spirit from the Father from that of the Son from the Father by saying that the latter is by generation, but that of the Spirit by procession (<span lang="grc">ἐκπρόρευσις</span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> a matter on which there is no dispute between East and West, as shown also by the Latin Father <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>, who wrote that although biblical exegetes had not adequately discussed the individuality of the Holy Spirit,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>they predicate Him to be the Gift of God, [and they infer] God not to give a gift inferior to Himself. [From that, they] predicate the Holy Spirit neither as begotten, like the Son, of the Father; [ ] nor [ ] of the Son, [ and] they do not affirm Him to owe that which He is to no one, [except] to the Father, [ ] lest we should establish two Beginnings without beginning [ ] which would be an assertion at once [ ] false and [ ] absurd, and one proper not to the catholic faith, but to the error of [<a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeism</a>].<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Nyssa" title="Gregory of Nyssa">Gregory of Nyssa</a> stated:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The one (i.e. the Son) is directly from the First and the other (i.e., the Spirit) is through the one who is directly from the First (<span lang="grc">τὸ δὲ ἐκ τοῦ προσεχῶς ἐκ τοῦ πρώτου</span>) with the result that the Only-begotten remains the Son and does not negate the Spirit's being from the Father since the middle position of the Son both protects His distinction as Only-begotten and does not exclude the Spirit from His natural relation to the Father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alexandrian_Fathers">Alexandrian Fathers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Alexandrian Fathers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cyril_of_Alexandria" title="Cyril of Alexandria">Cyril of Alexandria</a> provides "a host of quotations that seemingly speak of the Spirit's 'procession' from both the Father and the Son". In these passages he uses the Greek verbs <span lang="grc">προϊέναι</span> (like the Latin <i><span lang="la">procedere</span></i>) and <span lang="grc">προχεῖσθαι</span> (flow from), not the verb <span lang="grc">ἐκπορεύεσθαι</span>, the verb that appears in the Greek text of the Nicene Creed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201048.E2.80.9349_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201048.E2.80.9349-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Epiphanius of Salamis is stated by Bulgakov to present in his writings "a whole series of expressions to the effect that the Holy Spirit is from the Father and the Son, out of the Father and the Son, from the Father and out of the Son, from Both, from one and the same essence as the Father and the Son, and so on". Bulgakov concludes: "The patristic teaching of the fourth century lacks that exclusivity which came to characterize Orthodox theology after Photius under the influence of repulsion from the Filioque doctrine. Although we do not here find the pure <i>Filioque</i> that Catholic theologians find, we also do not find that opposition to the <i>Filioque</i> that became something of an Orthodox or, rather, anti-Catholic dogma."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulgakov200481.E2.80.9382_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulgakov200481.E2.80.9382-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epiphanius_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Epiphanius-24">[a]</a></sup></p>
<p>Regarding the Greek Fathers, whether Cappadocian or Alexandrian, there is, according to Siecienski, no citable basis for the claim historically made by both sides, that they explicitly either supported or denied the later theologies concerning the procession of the Spirit from the Son. However, they did enunciate important principles later invoked in support of one theology or the other. These included the insistence on the unique hypostatic properties of each Divine Person, in particular the Father's property of being, within the Trinity, the one cause, while they also recognized that the Persons, though distinct, cannot be separated, and that not only the sending of the Spirit to creatures but also the Spirit's eternal flowing forth (<span lang="grc">προϊέναι</span>) from the Father within the Trinity is "through the Son" (<span lang="grc">διὰ τοῦ Υἱοῦ</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201033.E2.80.9334_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201033.E2.80.9334-25">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_Fathers">Latin Fathers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Latin Fathers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Siecienski remarked that, "while the Greek fathers were still striving to find language capable of expressing the mysterious nature of the Son's relationship to the Spirit, Latin theologians, even during Cyril's lifetime, had already found their answer — the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Father and the Son (<i><span lang="la">ex Patre et Filio procedentem</span></i>). The degree to which this teaching was compatible with, or contradictory to, the emerging Greek tradition remains, sixteen centuries later, subject to debate."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201050_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201050-26">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before the creed of 381 became known in the West and even before it was adopted by the First Council of Constantinople, Christian writers in the West, of whom <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> (c. 160 – c. 220), <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> (347–420), <a href="/wiki/Ambrose" title="Ambrose">Ambrose</a> (c. 338 – 397) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustine" title="Augustine">Augustine</a> (354–430) are representatives, spoke of the Spirit as coming from the Father and the Son,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Double_Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Double_Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22-10">[10]</a></sup> while the expression "from the Father through the Son" is also found among them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TertullianAdversusPraxea4_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-TertullianAdversusPraxea4-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO.27CollinsFarrugia2015157_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO.27CollinsFarrugia2015157-29">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 3rd century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_province_of_Africa" title="Roman province of Africa">Roman province of Africa</a>, Tertullian emphasized, emphasizes that Father, Son and Holy Spirit all share a single divine substance, quality and power,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> which he conceives of as flowing forth from the Father and being transmitted by the Son to the Spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> Using the metaphor the root, the shoot, and the fruit; the spring, the river, and the stream; and the sun, the ray, and point of light for the unity with distinction in the Trinity, he adds, "The Spirit, then, is third from God and the Son,...."</p>
<p>In his arguments against <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arianism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marius_Victorinus" title="Marius Victorinus">Marius Victorinus</a> (c. 280–365) strongly connected the Son and the Spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the mid 4th century, <a href="/wiki/Hilary_of_Poitiers" title="Hilary of Poitiers">Hilary of Poitiers</a>, wrote of the Spirit "coming forth from the Father" and being "sent by the Son";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> as being "from the Father through the Son";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> and as "having the Father and the Son as his source";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> in another passage, Hilary points to <a class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=John+16:15–16:15&amp;version=nrsv" rel="nofollow">John 16:15</a> (where Jesus says: "All things that the Father has are mine; therefore I said that [the Spirit] shall take from what is mine and declare it to you"), and wonders aloud whether "to receive from the Son is the same thing as to proceed from the Father".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the late 4th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambrose_of_Milan" title="Ambrose of Milan">Ambrose of Milan</a> asserted that the Spirit "proceeds from (<i><span lang="la">procedit a</span></i>) the Father and the Son", without ever being separated from either.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> Ambrose adds, "[W]ith You, Almighty God, Your Son is the Fount of Life, that is, the Fount of the Holy Spirit. For the Spirit is life...." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>"None of these writers, however, makes the Spirit's mode of origin the object of special reflection; all are concerned, rather, to emphasize the equality of status of all three divine persons as God, and all acknowledge that the Father alone is the source of God's eternal being."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003-39">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_I" title="Pope Gregory I">Pope Gregory I</a>, in Gospel Homily 26, notes that the Son is "sent" by the Father both in the sense of an eternal generation and a temporal Incarnation. Thus, the Spirit is said to be "sent" by the Son from the Father both as to an eternal procession and a temporal mission. "The sending of the Spirit is that procession by which It proceeds from the Father and the Son."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> In his <a href="/wiki/Commentary_on_Job" title="Commentary on Job"><i>Moralia in Iob</i></a>, initially composed while he was <i>apocrisarius</i> at the imperial court of Constantinople and later edited while Pope of Rome, Gregory wrote, "But the Mediator of God and men, the Man Christ Jesus, in all things has Him (the Holy Spirit) both always and continually present. For the same Spirit even in substance is brought forth from Him (<i>quia et ex illo isdem Spiritus per substantiam profertur</i>.) And thus, though He (the Spirit) abides in the holy Preachers, He is justly said to abide in the Mediator in a special manner, for that in them He abides of grace for a particular object, but in Him He abides substantially for all ends."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> Later in his <i>Dialogues,</i> Gregory I took the <i>Filioque</i> doctrine for granted when he quoted <a class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=John+16:7–16:7&amp;version=nrsv" rel="nofollow">John 16:7</a>,and asked: if "it is certain that the Paraclete Spirit always proceeds from the Father and the Son, why does the Son say that He is about to leave so that [the Spirit] who never leaves the Son might come?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> The text proposes an eternal procession from both Father and the Son by the use of the word "always" (<i><span lang="la">semper</span></i>). Gregory I's use of <i><span lang="la">recessurum</span></i> and <i><span lang="la">recedit</span></i> is also significant for the divine procession because although the Spirit always proceeds (<i><span lang="la">semper procedat</span></i>) from the Father and the Son, the Spirit never leaves (<i><span lang="la">numquam recedit</span></i>) the Son by this eternal procession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup><sup class="noprint selfreference">[<i><a href="/wiki/Talk:Filioque#Dialogues_of_Pope_Gregory_I_may_be_pseudepigraphical" title="Talk:Filioque">discuss</a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Roman_Catholic_Theologians">Modern Roman Catholic Theologians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Modern Roman Catholic Theologians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Yves_Congar" title="Yves Congar">Yves Congar</a> commented, "The walls of separation do not reach as high as heaven."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECongar198389_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECongar198389-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title='"Looking'>further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> And <a href="/wiki/Aidan_Nichols" title="Aidan Nichols">Aidan Nichols</a> remarked that "the <i>Filioque</i> controversy is, in fact, a casualty of the theological pluralism of the patristic Church", on the one hand the Latin and Alexandrian tradition, on the other the Cappadocian and later Byzantine tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENichols2010255_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENichols2010255-45">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nicene_and_Niceno-Constantinopolitan_Creeds">Nicene and Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creeds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Nicene and Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creeds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Council_of_Constantinople_381_BnF_MS_Gr510_fol355.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="587" data-file-width="403" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Council_of_Constantinople_381_BnF_MS_Gr510_fol355.jpg/220px-Council_of_Constantinople_381_BnF_MS_Gr510_fol355.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Council_of_Constantinople_381_BnF_MS_Gr510_fol355.jpg/330px-Council_of_Constantinople_381_BnF_MS_Gr510_fol355.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/73/Council_of_Constantinople_381_BnF_MS_Gr510_fol355.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Council_of_Constantinople_381_BnF_MS_Gr510_fol355.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First Council of Constantinople with halo-adorned Emperor <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a> (miniature in <i>Homilies of Gregory Nazianzus</i> (879–882), Bibliothèque nationale de France)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a></div>
<p>The original Nicene Creed – composed in Greek and adopted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_ecumenical_council" title="First ecumenical council">first ecumenical council</a>, Nicaea I (325) – ended with the words "and in the Holy Spirit" without defining the procession of the Holy Spirit. The procession of the Holy Spirit was defined in what is also called the Nicene Creed, or more accurately the Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed (NCC), which was also composed in Greek.</p>
<p>Traditionally, the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> is attributed to the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Constantinople" title="First Council of Constantinople">First Council of Constantinople</a> of 381, whose participants, primarily Eastern bishops,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPercival1900162_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPercival1900162-46">[45]</a></sup> met, decided issues (legates of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Damasus_I" title="Pope Damasus I">Pope Damasus I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly20095_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly20095-47">[46]</a></sup> were present). Only in the second half of the 6th century did the West recognize Constantinople I as ecumenical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="Galavotti is self published and in Italian, one of the existing sources may have this factoid. (November 2015)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Category:Articles_contradicting_other_articles" title="Category:Articles contradicting other articles"><span title="Pope Leo I received the Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed at Chalcedon I in 451. (December 2015)">contradictory</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> is not documented earlier than the Council of Chalcedon (451),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriceGaddis20053_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriceGaddis20053-49">[48]</a></sup> which referred to as "the creed ... of the 150 saintly fathers assembled in Constantinople" in its acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETanner199084_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETanner199084-50">[49]</a></sup> It was cited at Chalcedon I on instructions from the representative of the Emperor who chaired the meeting and who may have wished to present it as "a precedent for drawing up new creeds and definitions to supplement the Creed of Nicaea, as a way of getting round the ban on new creeds in" Ephesus I canon 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriceGaddis20053_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriceGaddis20053-49">[48]</a></sup> The <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> was recognized and received by Leo I at Chalcedon I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCC247_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-CCC247-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup> Scholars do not agree on the connection between Constantinople I and the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr>, which was not simply an expansion of the Creed of Nicaea, and was probably based on another traditional creed independent of the one from Nicaea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> is roughly equivalent to the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed#The_original_Nicene_Creed_of_325" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a> plus two additional articles: one on the Holy Spirit and another about the Church, baptism, and resurrection of the dead. For the full text of both creeds, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comparison_of_Nicene_Creeds_of_325_and_381" title="Comparison of Nicene Creeds of 325 and 381">Comparison between Creed of 325 and Creed of 381</a>.</p>
<p>The <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> article professes:</p>
<table style="margin: 1em auto 1em auto;">
<tr>
<td><span style="color:gray"><span lang="grc">Καὶ εἰς</span></span></td>
<td><span style="color:gray"><span lang="la">Et in</span></span></td>
<td><span style="color:gray">And in</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span style="color:gray"><span lang="grc">τὸ Πνεῦμα τὸ Ἅγιον</span>,</span></td>
<td><span style="color:gray"><span lang="la">Spiritum Sanctum</span>,</span></td>
<td><span style="color:gray">the Holy Spirit,</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span style="color:gray"><span lang="grc">τὸ κύριον, τὸ ζωοποιόν</span>,</span></td>
<td><span style="color:gray"><span lang="la">Dominum et vivificantem</span>,</span></td>
<td><span style="color:gray">the Lord, the giver of life,</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="grc">τὸ ἐκ τοῦ Πατρός ἐκπορευόμενον</span>,</td>
<td><span lang="la">qui ex Patre <strong>⟨Filioque⟩</strong> procedit</span></td>
<td>who proceeds from the Father <strong>⟨and the Son⟩</strong>.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span style="color:gray"><span lang="grc">τὸ σὺν Πατρὶ καὶ Υἱῷ</span></span></td>
<td><span style="color:gray"><span lang="la">Qui cum Patre et Filio</span></span></td>
<td><span style="color:gray">With the Father and the Son</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span style="color:gray"><span lang="grc">συμπροσκυνούμενον καὶ συνδοξαζόμενον</span>,</span></td>
<td><span style="color:gray"><span lang="la">simul adoratur et conglorificatur</span>;</span></td>
<td><span style="color:gray">he is worshipped and glorified.</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span style="color:gray"><span lang="grc">τὸ λαλῆσαν διὰ τῶν προφητῶν</span>.</span></td>
<td><span style="color:gray"><span lang="la">qui locutus est per prophetas</span>.</span></td>
<td><span style="color:gray">He has spoken through the Prophets.</span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>It speaks of the Holy Spirit "proceeding from the Father" – a phrase based on <a class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=John+15:26–15:26&amp;version=nrsv" rel="nofollow">John 15:26</a>.</p>
<p>The Greek word <span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%90%CE%BA%CF%80%CE%BF%CF%81%CE%B5%CF%85%CF%8C%CE%BC%CE%B5%CE%BD%CE%BF%CE%BD" title="wikt:ἐκπορευόμενον">ἐκπορευόμενον</a></span> (<i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration">ekporeuomenon</span></i>) refers to the ultimate source from which the proceeding occurs, but the Latin verb <i><span lang="la"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/procedere" title="wikt:procedere">procedere</a></span></i> (and the corresponding terms used to translate it into other languages) can apply also to proceeding through a mediate channel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThiselton2013400_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThiselton2013400-54">[53]</a></sup> Frederick Bauerschmidt notes that what Medieval theologians disregarded as minor objections about ambiguous terms, was in fact an "insufficient understanding of the semantic difference" between the Greek and Latin terms in both the East and the West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBauerschmidt200598_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBauerschmidt200598-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[b]</a></sup> The West used the more generic Latin term <i><span lang="la">procedere</span></i> (to move forward; to come forth) which is more synonymous with the Greek term <span lang="grc">προϊέναι</span> (<i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration">proienai</span></i>) than the more specific Greek term <span lang="grc">ἐκπορεύεσθαι</span> (<i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration">ekporeuesthai</span></i>, "to issue forth as from an origin").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBauerschmidt200598_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBauerschmidt200598-55">[54]</a></sup> The West traditionally used one term and the East traditionally used two terms to convey arguably equivalent and complementary meaning, that is, <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration">ekporeuesthai</span></i> from the Father and <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration">proienai</span></i> from the Son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBauerschmidt2005Thiselton2013_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBauerschmidt2005Thiselton2013-57">[55]</a></sup> Moreover, the more generic Latin term, <i><span lang="la">procedere</span></i>, does not have "the added implication of the starting-point of that movement; thus it is used to translate a number of other Greek theological terms."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003-39">[38]</a></sup> It is used as the Latin equivalent, in the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a>, of not only <span lang="grc">ἐκπορεύεσθαι</span>, but also <span lang="grc">ἔρχεσθαι, προέρχεσθαι, προσέρχεσθαι</span>, and <span lang="grc">προβαίνω</span> (four times) and is used of Jesus' originating from God in <a class="external text" href="http://www.esvbible.org/John+8:42" rel="nofollow">John 8:42</a>, although at that time Greek <span lang="grc">ἐκπορεύεσθαι</span> was already beginning to designate the Holy Spirit's manner of originating from the Father as opposed to that of the Son (<span lang="grc">γέννησις</span> — being born).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201059_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201059-58">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_Ecumenical_Council">Third Ecumenical Council</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Third Ecumenical Council">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ephesus" title="Council of Ephesus">Council of Ephesus</a></div>
<p>The third ecumenical council, Ephesus I (431), it quoted the creed in its 325 form, not in that of 381,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPercival1900.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fnpnf214.x.xvi.x.html_231b.5D_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPercival1900.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fnpnf214.x.xvi.x.html_231b.5D-59">[57]</a></sup> decreed in Ephesus I canon 7 that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[ ] it is unlawful [ ] to bring forward, or to write, or to compose a different [ ] Faith as a rival to that established by the [ ] Fathers assembled [ ] in Nicæa. [ ] those who [ ] compose a different faith, or to introduce or offer it to persons desiring to turn to the acknowledgment of the truth, whether from Heathenism or from Judaism, or from any heresy whatsoever, shall be deposed, if they be bishops or clergymen; [ ] and if they be laymen, they shall be anathematized. [ ]<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPercival1900.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fnpnf214.x.xvi.x.html_231b.5D_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPercival1900.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fnpnf214.x.xvi.x.html_231b.5D-59">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[c]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Ephesus I canon 7 was cited at the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Ephesus" title="Second Council of Ephesus">Second Council of Ephesus</a> (449) and at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> (451), and was echoed in the Chalcedon definition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriceGaddis20058.2C_111_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriceGaddis20058.2C_111-61">[58]</a></sup> This account in the 2005 publication concerning the citing by Eutyches of Ephesus I canon 7 in his defence was confirmed by Stephen H. Webb in his 2011 book <i>Jesus Christ, Eternal God</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Stay_on_topic" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles"><span title="The sentence near this tag may contain information that is not relevant to the article's main topic. (November 2015)">relevant?</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Filioque" title="Talk:Filioque">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Ephesus I canon 7, against additions to the Creed of Nicaea, is used as a polemic against the addition of <i>Filioque</i> to the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28b.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fbible-history.2Fchurch-history.2Ffifth-century_Fifth_Century.5D.22_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28b.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fbible-history.2Fchurch-history.2Ffifth-century_Fifth_Century.5D.22-63">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup> In any case, while Ephesus I canon 7 forbade setting up a different creed as a rival to that of Nicaea I, it was the creed attributed to Constantinople I that was adopted liturgically in the East and later a Latin variant was adopted in the West. The form of this creed that the West adopted had two additions: "God from God" (<i><span lang="la">Deum de Deo</span></i>) and "and the Son" (<i><span lang="la">Filioque</span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENichols2010254_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENichols2010254-65">[62]</a></sup> Strictly speaking, Ephesus I canon 7 applies "only to the formula to be used in the reception of converts."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriceGaddis2005323_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriceGaddis2005323-66">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Philippe_Labb%C3%A9" title="Philippe Labbé">Philippe Labbé</a> remarked that Ephesus I canons 7 and 8 are omitted in some collections of canons and that the collection of <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_Exiguus" title="Dionysius Exiguus">Dionysius Exiguus</a> omitted all the Ephesus I canons, apparently considered that they did not concern the Church as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPercival1900.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fnpnf214.x.xvi.ix.html_231a.5D_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPercival1900.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fnpnf214.x.xvi.ix.html_231a.5D-67">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fourth_Ecumenical_Council">Fourth Ecumenical Council</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Fourth Ecumenical Council">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a></div>
<p>At the fourth ecumenical council, Chalcedon I (451), both the Nicene Creed of 325 and the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> were read, the former at the request of a bishop, the latter, against the protests of the bishops, on the initiative of the emperor's representative, "doubtless motivated by the need to find a precedent for drawing up new creeds and definitions to supplement the Creed of Nicaea, as a way of getting round the ban on new creeds in" Ephesus I canon 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriceGaddis20053_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriceGaddis20053-49">[48]</a></sup> The acts of Chalcedon I defined that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[ ] no one shall [ ] bring forward a different faith [ ], nor to write, nor to put together, nor to excogitate, nor to teach it to others. [Those who] either [ ] put together another faith, or [ ] bring forward or [ ] teach or [ ] deliver a different Creed [ ] to [those who] wish to be converted [ ] from the Gentiles, or Jews or any heresy whatever, if they be Bishops or clerics let them be deposed, [ ] but if they be monks or laics: let them be anathematized. [ ]<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPercival1900.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fnpnf214.xi.xiii.html_265.5D_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPercival1900.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fnpnf214.xi.xiii.html_265.5D-68">[65]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_earliest_use_in_the_Creed">Possible earliest use in the Creed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Possible earliest use in the Creed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some scholars claim that the earliest example of the <i>Filioque</i> clause in the East is contained in the West Syriac recension of the profession of faith of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> formulated at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Seleucia-Ctesiphon" title="Council of Seleucia-Ctesiphon">Council of Seleucia-Ctesiphon</a> in Persia in 410.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriceGaddis2005193_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriceGaddis2005193-69">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[d]</a></sup> This Council was held some twenty years before the <a href="/wiki/Nestorian_Schism" title="Nestorian Schism">Nestorian Schism</a> that caused later split between the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> and the Church in the Roman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup> Since wording of that recension ("who is from the Father and the Son") does not contain any mention of the term "procession" or any of the other particular terms that would describe relations between Father, Son and the Holy Spirit, the previously mentioned claim for the "earliest use" of <i>Filioque</i> clause is not universally accepted by the scholars. Furthermore, another recension that is preserved in the East Syriac sources of the Church of the East, contains only wording: "And in the Holy Spirit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrock1985133_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrock1985133-72">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Various professions of faith confessed the doctrine during the patristic age. The <i><span lang="la">Fides Damasi</span></i> (380 or 5th century), a profession of faith attributed to Pseudo-Damasus or <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a>, includes a formula of the doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._71Kelly2014360_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._71Kelly2014360-73">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[e]</a></sup> The <i><span lang="la">Symbolum Toletanum I</span></i> (400), a profession of faith legislated by the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Toledo" title="First Council of Toledo">Toledo I synod</a>, includes a formula of the doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012-76">[71]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Athanasian_Creed" title="Athanasian Creed">Athanasian Creed</a> (5th century), a profession of faith attributed to Pseudo-Athanasius, includes a formula of the doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995DH2012n._75_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995DH2012n._75-77">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The generally accepted first found insertion of the term <i>Filioque</i> into the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr>, in Western Christianity, is in acts of the <a href="/wiki/Third_Council_of_Toledo" title="Third Council of Toledo">Toledo III synod</a> (589),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012160Louth2007142Kelly2014360.E2.80.93362_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012160Louth2007142Kelly2014360.E2.80.93362-78">[73]</a></sup> nearly two centuries later, but it may be a later interpolation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012160Kelly2014362_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012160Kelly2014362-79">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[f]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit">Procession of the Holy Spirit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Procession of the Holy Spirit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As early as the 4th century, a distinction was made, in connection with the Trinity, between the two Greek verbs <span lang="grc">ἐκπορεύεσθαι</span> (the verb used in the original Greek text of the 381 Nicene Creed) and <span lang="grc">προϊέναι</span>. <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Nazianzus" title="Gregory of Nazianzus">Gregory of Nazianzus</a> wrote: "The Holy Ghost is truly Spirit, coming forth (<span lang="grc">προϊέναι</span>) from the Father indeed, but not after the manner of the Son, for it is not by Generation but by Procession (<span lang="grc">ἐκπορεύεσθαι</span>)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>That the Holy Spirit "proceeds" from the Father and the Son in the sense of the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i><span lang="la">procedere</span></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <span lang="grc">προϊέναι</span> (as opposed to the Greek <span lang="grc">ἐκπορεύεσθαι</span>) was taught by the early 5th century by <a href="/wiki/Cyril_of_Alexandria" title="Cyril of Alexandria">Cyril of Alexandria</a> in the East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Double_Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Double_Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[77]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Athanasian_Creed" title="Athanasian Creed">Athanasian Creed</a>, probably composed as early as the mid 5th-century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[78]</a></sup> and a dogmatic epistle of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_I" title="Pope Leo I">Pope Leo I</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeoI447_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeoI447-85">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[g]</a></sup> who declared in 446 that the Holy Spirit proceeds from both Father and Son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCC247_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-CCC247-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the Eastern Fathers were aware that the procession of the Holy Spirit from the Father and the Son was taught in the West, they did not generally regard it as heretical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDulles199532.2C_40_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDulles199532.2C_40-87">[80]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Bulgakov" title="Sergei Bulgakov">Sergei Bulgakov</a> "a whole series of Western writers, including popes who are venerated as saints by the Eastern church, confess the procession of the Holy Spirit also from the Son; and it is even more striking that there is virtually no disagreement with this theory."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulgakov200490_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulgakov200490-88">[81]</a></sup> In 447, Leo I taught it in a letter to a Spanish bishop and an anti-<a href="/wiki/Priscillianism" title="Priscillianism">Priscillianist</a> council held the same year proclaimed it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeoI447_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-LeoI447-85">[79]</a></sup> The argument was taken a crucial step further in 867 by the affirmation in the East that the Holy Spirit proceeds not merely "from the Father" but "from the Father <i>alone</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuretzki20098_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuretzki20098-89">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulgakov200495_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulgakov200495-90">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Filioque</i> was inserted into the Creed as an anti-Arian addition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarthaler2001248.E2.80.93249_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarthaler2001248.E2.80.93249-91">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIrvinSunquist2001340_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrvinSunquist2001340-92">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDix2005485.E2.80.93488_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDix2005485.E2.80.93488-93">[86]</a></sup> by Toledo III, at which King <a href="/wiki/Reccared_I" title="Reccared I">Reccared I</a> and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arians" title="Arians">Arians</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Visigothic_Kingdom" title="Visigothic Kingdom">Visigothic Kingdom</a> converted to orthodox, catholic Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHinson1995220_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHinson1995220-94">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouth2007142_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouth2007142-95">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[h]</a></sup> The Toledo XI synod (675) included the doctrine but not the term in its profession of faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._527_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._527-97">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other Toledo synods "to affirm Trinitarian consubstantiality" between 589 and 693.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995DH2012nn._470.2C_485.2C_490.2C_527.2C_568_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995DH2012nn._470.2C_485.2C_490.2C_527.2C_568-98">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Filioque</i> clause was confirmed by subsequent synods in Toledo and soon spread throughout the West, not only in Spain, but also in <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Francia</a>, after <a href="/wiki/Clovis_I" title="Clovis I">Clovis I</a>, king of the <a href="/wiki/Salian_Franks" title="Salian Franks">Salian Franks</a>, converted to Christianity in 496; and in England, where the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Hatfield" title="Council of Hatfield">Council of Hatfield</a> (680), presided over by Archbishop of Canterbury <a href="/wiki/Theodore_of_Tarsus" title="Theodore of Tarsus">Theodore of Tarsus</a>, a Greek,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201088_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201088-99">[91]</a></sup> imposed the doctrine as a response to <a href="/wiki/Monothelitism" title="Monothelitism">Monothelitism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlested2011_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlested2011-100">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, while the doctrine was taught in Rome, the term was not professed liturgically in the until 1014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a> the Latin verb <i><span lang="la">procedere</span></i>, which appears in the <i>Filioque</i> passage of the Creed in Latin, is used to translate several Greek verbs. While one of those verbs, <span lang="grc">ἐκπορεύεσθαι</span>, the one in the corresponding phrase in the Creed in Greek, "was beginning to take on a particular meaning in Greek theology designating the Spirit's unique mode of coming-to-be ... <i><span lang="la">procedere</span></i> had no such connotations".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201059_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201059-58">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Hilary_of_Poitiers" title="Hilary of Poitiers">Hilary of Poitiers</a> is often cited as one of "the chief patristic source(s) for the Latin teaching on the filioque", Siecienski says that "there is also reason for questioning Hilary's support for the <i>Filioque</i> as later theology would understand it, especially given the ambiguous nature of (Hilary's) language as it concerns the procession."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201053_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201053-101">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, a number of <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Church</a> Fathers of the 4th and 5th centuries explicitly speak of the Holy Spirit as proceeding "from the Father and the Son", the phrase in the present Latin version of the Nicene Creed. Examples are what is called the creed of Pope Damasus I,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201057_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201057-102">[94]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambrose_of_Milan" title="Ambrose of Milan">Ambrose of Milan</a> ("one of the earliest witnesses to the explicit affirmation of the Spirit's procession from the Father <i>and</i> the Son"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201057_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201057-102">[94]</a></sup> Augustine of Hippo (whose writings on the Trinity "became the foundation of subsequent Latin trinitarian theology and later served as the foundation for the doctrine of the <i>filioque</i>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201059_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201059-58">[56]</a></sup> and Leo I, who qualified as "impious" those who say "there is not one who begat, another who is begotten, another who proceeded from both [alius qui de utroque processerit]"; he also accepted the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a>, with its reaffirmation of the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> in its original "from the Father" form,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201063.E2.80.9364_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201063.E2.80.9364-103">[95]</a></sup> as much later did his successor <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_III" title="Pope Leo III">Pope Leo III</a> who professed his faith in the teaching expressed by the <i>Filioque</i>, while opposing its inclusion in the Creed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlested2011_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlested2011-100">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thereafter, <a href="/wiki/Eucherius_of_Lyon" title="Eucherius of Lyon">Eucherius of Lyon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gennadius_of_Massilia" title="Gennadius of Massilia">Gennadius of Massilia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boethius" title="Boethius">Boethius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agnellus,_Bishop_of_Ravenna" title="Agnellus, Bishop of Ravenna">Agnellus, Bishop of Ravenna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cassiodorus" title="Cassiodorus">Cassiodorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tours" title="Gregory of Tours">Gregory of Tours</a> are witnesses that the idea <i>that</i> the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Son was well established as part of the (Western) Church's faith, before Latin theologians began to concern themselves about <i>how</i> the Spirit proceeds from the Son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201064.E2.80.9366_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201064.E2.80.9366-104">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_I" title="Pope Gregory I">Pope Gregory I</a> is usually counted as teaching the Spirit's procession from the Son, although Byzantine theologians, quoting from Greek translations of his work rather than the original, present him as a witness against it, and although he sometimes speaks of the Holy Spirit as proceeding from the Father without mentioning the Son. Siecienski says that, in view of the widespread acceptance by then that the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Father and the Son, it would be strange if Gregory did not advocate the teaching, "even if he did not understand the <i>filioque</i> as later Latin theology would — that is, in terms of a 'double procession'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201070.E2.80.9371_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201070.E2.80.9371-105">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".22From_the_Father_through_the_Son.22">"From the Father through the Son"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title='Edit section: "From the Father through the Son"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Church Fathers also use the phrase "from the Father through the Son".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TertullianAdversusPraxea4_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-TertullianAdversusPraxea4-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[98]</a></sup> Cyril of Alexandria, who undeniably several times states that the Holy Spirit issues from the Father <i>and</i> the Son, also speaks of the Holy Spirit coming from the Father <i>through</i> the Son, two different expressions that for him are complementary: the procession of the Holy Spirit from the Father does not exclude the Son's mediation and the Son receives from the Father a participation in the Holy Spirit's coming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoulnois2003106.E2.80.93108_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoulnois2003106.E2.80.93108-107">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[i]</a></sup> He was attacked by <a href="/wiki/Theodoret" title="Theodoret">Theodoret</a> for saying the Holy Spirit has his existence "either from the Son or through the Son", but continued to use both forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECongar198335_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECongar198335-109">[100]</a></sup> The Roman Catholic Church accepts both phrases, and considers that they do not affect the reality of the same faith and instead express the same truth in slightly different ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCC248_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-CCC248-110">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies1993205.E2.80.93206_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies1993205.E2.80.93206-111">[102]</a></sup> The influence of Augustine of Hippo made the phrase "proceeds from the Father through the Son" popular throughout the West,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies1987_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies1987-112">[103]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> but, while used also in the East, "through the Son" was later, according to Philip Schaff, dropped or rejected by some as being nearly equivalent to "from the Son" or "and the Son".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaff1885.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fhcc4.i.xi.iii.html_.C2.A7108_II.5D_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaff1885.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fhcc4.i.xi.iii.html_.C2.A7108_II.5D-113">[104]</a></sup> Others spoke of the Holy Spirit proceeding "from the Father", as in the text of the Nicaeno-Constantinopolitan Creed, which "did not state that the Spirit proceeds from the Father <i>alone</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO.27CollinsFarrugia2015158_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO.27CollinsFarrugia2015158-114">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Eastern_opposition">First Eastern opposition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: First Eastern opposition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maximus_Confessor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="442" data-file-width="315" height="309" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Maximus_Confessor.jpg/220px-Maximus_Confessor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/31/Maximus_Confessor.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maximus_Confessor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Maximus the Confessor</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first recorded objection by a representative of Eastern Christianity against the Western belief that the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Father and the Son occurred when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Paul_II_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Paul II of Constantinople">Patriarch Paul II of Constantinople</a> (<abbr title="reign">r.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 642</span> –<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 653</span>) made accusations against either <a href="/wiki/Pope_Theodore_I" title="Pope Theodore I">Pope Theodore I</a> (<abbr title="reign">r.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 642</span> –<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 649</span>) or <a href="/wiki/Pope_Martin_I" title="Pope Martin I">Pope Martin I</a> (<abbr title="reign">r.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 649</span> –<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 653</span>) for using the expression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulgakov200491.E2.80.9392_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulgakov200491.E2.80.9392-115">[106]</a></sup> Theodore I excommunicated Paul II in 647 for <a href="/wiki/Monothelitism" title="Monothelitism">Monothelitism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorwich199799_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorwich199799-116">[107]</a></sup> In response to the attack by Paul, Maximus the Confessor, a Greek opponent of Monothelitism, declared that it was wrong to condemn the Roman use of "and the Son" because the Romans "have produced the unanimous evidence of the Latin Fathers, and also of <a href="/wiki/Cyril_of_Alexandria" title="Cyril of Alexandria">Cyril of Alexandria</a> [...] On the basis of these texts, they have shown that they have not made the Son the cause of the Spirit — they know in fact that the Father is the only cause of the Son and the Spirit, the one by begetting and the other by procession — but that they have manifested the procession through him and have thus shown the unity and identity of the essence." He also indicated that the differences between the Latin and Greek languages were an obstacle to mutual understanding, since "they cannot reproduce their idea in a language and in words that are foreign to them as they can in their mother-tongue, just as we too cannot do".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_of_authenticity">Claims of authenticity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Claims of authenticity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the end of 8th and the beginning of 9th century, the Church of Rome was faced with an unusual challenge regarding the use of Filioque clause. Among the Church leaders in Frankish Kingdom of that time a notion was developing that Filioque clause was in fact an authentic part of the original Creed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201092_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201092-118">[109]</a></sup> Trying to deal with that problem and its potentially dangerous consequences, the Church of Rome found itself in the middle of a widening rift between its own Daughter-Church in Frankish Kingdom and Sister-Churches of the East. Popes of that time, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pope_Hadrian_I" title="Pope Hadrian I">Hadrian I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_III" title="Pope Leo III">Leo III</a>, had to face various challenges while trying to find solutions that would preserve the unity of the Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff199638_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff199638-119">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>First signs of the problems were starting to show by the end of the reign of Frankish king <a href="/wiki/Pepin_the_Short" title="Pepin the Short">Pepin the Short</a> (751-768). Use of the <i>Filioque</i> clause in the Frankish Kingdom led to controversy with envoys of the Byzantine Emperor <a href="/wiki/Constantine_V" title="Constantine V">Constantine V</a> at the Synod of Gentilly (767).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaas1909_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaas1909-120">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHinson1995315_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHinson1995315-121">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201090_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201090-122">[113]</a></sup> As the practice of chanting the interpolated Latin <i><span lang="la">Credo</span></i> at <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)" title="Mass (liturgy)">Mass</a> spread in the West, the <i>Filioque</i> became a part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_Rite" title="Latin Rite">Latin Rite</a> liturgy throughout the Frankish Kingdom. The practice of chanting the Creed was adopted in Charlemagne's court by the end of 8th century and spread through all of his realms, including some northern parts of Italy, but not the Rome, where its use was not accepted until 1014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDix2005485.E2.80.93488_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDix2005485.E2.80.93488-93">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouth2007142_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouth2007142-95">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Serious problems erupted in 787 after the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">Second Council of Nicaea</a> when Charlemagne accused the Patriarch <a href="/wiki/Tarasios_of_Constantinople" title="Tarasios of Constantinople">Tarasios of Constantinople</a> of infidelity to the faith of the First Council of Nicaea, allegedly because he had not professed the procession of the Holy Spirit from the Father "and the Son", but only "through the Son". Pope Hadrian rejected those accusations and tried to explain to the Frankish king that pneumatology of Tarasios was in accordance with the teachings of the holy Fathers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201091_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201091-123">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENichols2010237_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENichols2010237-124">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[j]</a></sup> Surprisingly, efforts of the pope had no effect.</p>
<p>True scale of the problem became evident during the following years. The Frankish view of the <i>Filioque</i> was emphasized again in the <i><a href="/wiki/Libri_Carolini" title="Libri Carolini">Libri Carolini</a></i>, composed around 791-793.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[k]</a></sup> Openly arguing that the word <i>Filioque</i> was part of the Creed of 381, the authors of <i>Libri Carolini</i> demonstrated not only the surprising lack of basic knowledge but also the lack of will to receive right advice and council from the Mother-Church in Rome. Frankish theologians reaffirmed the notion that the Spirit proceeds from the Father and the Son, and rejected as inadequate the teaching that the Spirit proceeds from the Father <i>through the Son</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201091-93_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201091-93-127">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENichols2010237_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENichols2010237-124">[115]</a></sup> That claim was both erroneous and dangerous for the preservation of the unity of the Church.</p>
<p>In those days, another theological problem appeared to be closely connected with the use of Filioque in the West. In the late 8th century, a controversy arose between Bishop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elipandus_of_Toledo" title="Elipandus of Toledo">Elipandus of Toledo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beatus_of_Li%C3%A9bana" title="Beatus of Liébana">Beatus of Liébana</a> over the former's teaching (which has been called <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Adoptionism" title="Spanish Adoptionism">Spanish Adoptionism</a>) that Christ in his humanity was the adoptive son of God. Elipandus was supported by Bishop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Felix_of_Urgel" title="Felix of Urgel">Felix of Urgel</a>. In 785, Pope Hadrian I condemned the teaching of Elipandus. In 791, Felix appealed to Charlemagne in defense of the Spanish Adoptionist teaching, sending him a tract outlining it. He was condemned at the Synod of Regensburg (792) and was sent to Pope Hadrian in Rome, where he made of profession of orthodox faith, but returned to Spain and there reaffirmed Adoptionism. Elipandus wrote to the bishops of the territories controlled by Charlemagne in defence of his teaching, which was condemned at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Frankfurt" title="Council of Frankfurt">Council of Frankfurt</a> (794) and at the Synod of <a href="/wiki/Friuli" title="Friuli">Friuli</a> (796). The controversy encouraged those who rejected Adoptionism to introduce into the liturgy the use of the Creed, with the <i>Filioque</i>, to profess belief that Christ was the Son from eternity, not adopted as a son at his baptism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201093-94_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201093-94-128">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDales201361.E2.80.9367_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDales201361.E2.80.9367-129">[118]</a></sup></p>
