<p>At the Synod of Friuli, <a href="/wiki/Paulinus_II_of_Aquileia" title="Paulinus II of Aquileia">Paulinus II of Aquileia</a> stated that the insertion of <i>Filioque</i> in the 381 Creed of the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Constantinople" title="First Council of Constantinople">First Council of Constantinople</a> was no more a violation of the prohibition of new creeds than were the insertions into the 325 Creed of the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Council of Nicaea</a> that were done by the First Council of Constantinople itself. What was forbidden, he said, was adding or removing something "craftily ... contrary to the sacred intentions of the fathers", not a council's addition that could be shown to be in line with the intentions of the Fathers and the faith of the ancient Church. Actions such as that of the First Council of Contantinople were sometimes called for in order to clarify the faith and do away with heresies that appear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201093_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201093-130">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENichols2010238_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENichols2010238-131">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly2014364_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly2014364-132">[121]</a></sup> The views of Paulinus show that some advocates of Filioque clause were quite aware of the fact that it actually was not part of the Creed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENichols2010238_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENichols2010238-131">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>Political events that followed additionally complicated the issue. According to <a href="/wiki/John_Meyendorff" title="John Meyendorff">John Meyendorff</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff199641.E2.80.9343.2C_195.E2.80.93197_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff199641.E2.80.9343.2C_195.E2.80.93197-133">[122]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/John_Romanides" title="John Romanides">John Romanides</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romanides1_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Romanides1-134">[123]</a></sup> the Frankish efforts to get new <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_III" title="Pope Leo III">Pope Leo III</a> to approve the addition of <i>Filioque</i> to the Creed were due to a desire of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, who in 800 had been crowned in Rome as Emperor, to find grounds for accusations of heresy against the East. The Pope's refusal to approve the interpolation of the <i>Filioque</i> into the Creed avoided arousing a conflict between East and West about this matter. During his reign (<abbr title="reign">r.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 795</span> –<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 816</span>), and for another two centuries, there was no Creed at all in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_rite" title="Roman rite">Roman rite</a> Mass.</p>
<p>Reasons for the continuing refusal of the Frankish Church to adopt the positions of the Church of Rome on necessity of leaving Filioque outside of Creed remained unknown. Faced with another endorsement of the Filioque clause at the Frankish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Aachen_(809)" title="Council of Aachen (809)">Council of Aachen (809)</a> pope Leo III denied his approval and publicly posted the Creed in Rome without the Filioque, written in Greek and Latin on two silver plaques, in defense of the Orthodox Faith (810) stating his opposition to the addition of the <i>Filioque</i> into the Creed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Filioque.22_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Filioque.22-135">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201091-93_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201091-93-127">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENichols2010238-239_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENichols2010238-239-136">[125]</a></sup> Although Leo III did not disapprove the <i>Filioque</i> doctrine, the Pope strongly believed the clause should not be included into the Creed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaas1909_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaas1909-120">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Filioque.22_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Filioque.22-135">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[l]</a></sup> In spite of the efforts of the Church of Rome, the acceptance of the Filioque clause in the Creed of the Frankish Church proved to be irreversible.</p>
<p>In 808 or 809 apparent controversy arose in Jerusalem between the Greek monks of one monastery and the Frankish Benedictine monks of another: the Greeks reproached the latter for, among other things, singing the creed with the <i>Filioque</i> included.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003_39-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmaus1975_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmaus1975-138">[126]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarnack1898.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fharnack.2Fdogma5.ii.ii.i.vi.iv.html_ch._6_.C2.A72.5D_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarnack1898.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fharnack.2Fdogma5.ii.ii.i.vi.iv.html_ch._6_.C2.A72.5D-139">[127]</a></sup> In response, the theology of the <i>Filioque</i> was expressed in the 809 local <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Aachen" title="Council of Aachen">Council of Aachen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003_39-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarnack1898.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fharnack.2Fdogma5.ii.ii.i.vi.iv.html_ch._6_.C2.A72.5D_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarnack1898.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fharnack.2Fdogma5.ii.ii.i.vi.iv.html_ch._6_.C2.A72.5D-139">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBray1983121_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBray1983121-140">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photian_controversy">Photian controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Photian controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Around 860 the controversy over the <i>Filioque</i> broke out in the course of the disputes between Patriarch <a href="/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Photios I of Constantinople">Photios I of Constantinople</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Ignatius_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Ignatius of Constantinople">Patriarch Ignatius of Constantinople</a>. In 867 Photius was Patriarch of Constantinople and issued an <i>Encyclical to the Eastern Patriarchs</i>, and called a council in Constantinople in which he charged the Western Church with <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a> and schism because of differences in practices, in particular for the <i>Filioque</i> and the authority of the Papacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaff1885.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fhcc4.i.v.iv.html_.C2.A770.5D_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaff1885.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fhcc4.i.v.iv.html_.C2.A770.5D-141">[129]</a></sup> This moved the issue from jurisdiction and custom to one of dogma. This council declared Pope Nicholas anathema, excommunicated and deposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Photius.22_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Photius.22-142">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Photius excluded not only "and the Son" but also "through the Son" with regard to the eternal procession of the Holy Spirit: for him "through the Son" applied only to the temporal mission of the Holy Spirit (the sending in time).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChadwick2003154_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChadwick2003154-143">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaff1885.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fhcc4.i.xi.iii.html_.C2.A7108_II.5D_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaff1885.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fhcc4.i.xi.iii.html_.C2.A7108_II.5D-113">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff1986.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.myriobiblos.gr.2Ftexts.2Fenglish.2FMeyendorff_12.html_.C2.A72.5D_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff1986.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.myriobiblos.gr.2Ftexts.2Fenglish.2FMeyendorff_12.html_.C2.A72.5D-144">[132]</a></sup> He maintained that the eternal procession of the Holy Spirit is "from the Father <i>alone</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[133]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The quotation near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source to ensure that it is accurate, not taken out of context, and/or actually exists. (December 2015)">verify</span></a></i>]</sup> This phrase was verbally a novelty,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPapadakis1997113_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPapadakis1997113-146">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELossky2003168_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELossky2003168-147">[135]</a></sup> however, Orthodox theologians generally hold that in substance the phrase is only a reaffirmation of traditional teaching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPapadakis1997113_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPapadakis1997113-146">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELossky2003168_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELossky2003168-147">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Bulgakov" title="Sergei Bulgakov">Sergei Bulgakov</a>, on the other hand, declared that Photius's doctrine itself "represents a sort of novelty for the Eastern church".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulgakov2004144_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulgakov2004144-148">[136]</a></sup> Bulgakov writes: "The Cappadocians expressed only one idea: the monarchy of the Father and, consequently, the procession of the Holy Spirit precisely from the Father. They never imparted to this idea, however, the exclusiveness that it acquired in the epoch of the Filioque disputes after Photius, in the sense of <i>ek monou tou Patros</i> (from the Father alone)";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulgakov200480_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulgakov200480-149">[137]</a></sup> Nichols summarized that, "Bulgakov finds it amazing that with all his erudition Photius did not see that the 'through the Spirit' of Damascene and others constituted a different theology from his own, just as it is almost incomprehensible to find him trying to range the Western Fathers and popes on his Monopatrist side."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENichols2005157_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENichols2005157-150">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>Photius's importance endured in regard to relations between East and West. He is recognized as a saint by the Eastern Orthodox Church and his line of criticism has often been echoed later, making reconciliation between East and West difficult.</p>
<p>At least three councils – <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Constantinople_(867)" title="Council of Constantinople (867)">Council of Constantinople (867)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Council_of_Constantinople_(Roman_Catholic)" title="Fourth Council of Constantinople (Roman Catholic)">Fourth Council of Constantinople (Roman Catholic)</a> (869), and <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Council_of_Constantinople_(Eastern_Orthodox)" title="Fourth Council of Constantinople (Eastern Orthodox)">Fourth Council of Constantinople (Eastern Orthodox)</a> (879) – were held in Constantinople over the actions of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Michael_III" title="Michael III">Michael III</a> in deposing Ignatius and replacing him with Photius. The Council of Constantinople (867) was convened by Photius to address the question of Papal Supremacy over all of the churches and their patriarchs and the use of the <i>Filioque</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortescue1908147.E2.80.93148_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortescue1908147.E2.80.93148-151">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELouth2007171_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELouth2007171-152">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010103_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010103-154">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>The council of 867 was followed by the Fourth Council of Constantinople (Roman Catholic), in 869, which reversed the previous council and was promulgated by <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Rome" title="Diocese of Rome">Rome</a>. The Fourth Council of Constantinople (Eastern Orthodox), in 879, restored Photius to his see. It was attended by Western legates Cardinal Peter of St Chrysogonus, Paul Bishop of Ancona and Eugene Bishop of Ostia who approved its canons, but it is unclear whether it was ever promulgated by Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortescue1911_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortescue1911-155">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adoption_in_the_Roman_Rite">Adoption in the Roman Rite</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Adoption in the Roman Rite">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Latin liturgical use of the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> with the added term spread between the 8th and 11th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCC247_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-CCC247-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>It was only in 1014, at the request of King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_Germany" title="Henry II of Germany">Henry II of Germany</a>, who was in Rome for his coronation as <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> and was surprised by the different custom in force there, that <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_VIII" title="Pope Benedict VIII">Pope Benedict VIII</a>, who owed to Henry II his restoration to the papal throne after usurpation by <a href="/wiki/Antipope_Gregory_VI" title="Antipope Gregory VI">Antipope Gregory VI</a>, had the Creed, with the addition of <i>Filioque</i>, sung at Mass in Rome for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDix2005485.E2.80.93488_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDix2005485.E2.80.93488-93">[86]</a></sup> In some other places <i>Filioque</i> was incorporated in the Creed even later: in parts of southern Italy after the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Bari" title="Council of Bari">Council of Bari</a> in 1098<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kidd2013_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kidd2013-156">[144]</a></sup> and at Paris seemingly not even by 1240,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENichols199576_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENichols199576-157">[145]</a></sup> 34 years before the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Lyon" title="Second Council of Lyon">Second Council of Lyon</a> defined that the Holy Spirit "proceeds eternally from the Father and from the Son, not as from two principles but from a single principle, not by two spirations but by a single spiration".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._850_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._850-158">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETanner1990314_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETanner1990314-159">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since then the <i>Filioque</i> phrase has been included in the Creed throughout the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_Rite" title="Latin Rite">Latin Rite</a> except where <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> is used in the liturgy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[148]</a></sup> although it was never adopted by <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic_Churches" title="Eastern Catholic Churches">Eastern Catholic Churches</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brest_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brest-161">[149]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East.E2.80.93West_controversy">East–West controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: East–West controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a></div>
<p>Eastern opposition to the <i>Filioque</i> strengthened after the 11th century East–West Schism. According to the synodal edict, a Latin anathema, in the excommunication of 1054, against the Greeks included: "<span lang="la">ut Pneumatomachi sive Theomachi, Spiritus sancti ex Filio processionem ex symbolo absciderunt</span>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWill1861163_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWill1861163-162">[150]</a></sup> ("as pneumatomachi and theomachi, they have cut from the Creed the procession of the holy Spirit from the Son"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Translation" title="Wikipedia:Translation"><span title="This translation of a non-English text into English lacks translator's reference. (November 2015)">whose translation?</span></a></i>]</sup>). The Council of Constantinople, in a synodal edict, responded with anathemas against the Latins:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWill1861159_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWill1861159-163">[151]</a></sup> ("And besides all this, and quite unwilling to see that it is they claim that the Spirit proceeds from the Father, not [only], but also from the Son — as if they have no evidence of the evangelists of this, and if they do not have the dogma of the ecumenical council regarding this slander. For the Lord our God says, "even the Spirit of truth, which proceeds from the Father (John 15:26)". But parents say this new wickedness of the Spirit, who proceeds from the Father and the Son."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Translation" title="Wikipedia:Translation"><span title="This translation of a non-English text into English lacks translator's reference. (November 2015)">whose translation?</span></a></i>]</sup>)</p>
<p>Two councils that were held to heal the break discussed the question.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Lyon" title="Second Council of Lyon">Second Council of Lyon</a> (1274) accepted the profession of faith of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Michael_VIII_Palaiologos" title="Michael VIII Palaiologos">Michael VIII Palaiologos</a>: "We believe also ⟨in⟩ the Holy Spirit, fully, perfectly and truly God, proceeding from the Father and the Son, fully equal, of the same substance, equally almighty and equally eternal with the Father and the Son in all things."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._853_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._853-164">[152]</a></sup> and the Greek participants, including Patriarch <a href="/wiki/Joseph_I_of_Constantinople" title="Joseph I of Constantinople">Joseph I of Constantinople</a> sang the Creed three times with the <i>Filioque</i> clause. Most Byzantine Christians feeling disgust and recovering from the Latin Crusaders' conquest and betrayal, refused to accept the agreement made at Lyon with the Latins. Michael VIII was excommunicated by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Martin_IV" title="Pope Martin IV">Pope Martin IV</a> in November 1281,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[153]</a></sup> and later died, after which Patriarch Joseph I's successor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_John_XI_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch John XI of Constantinople">Patriarch John XI of Constantinople</a>, who had become convinced that the teaching of the Greek Fathers was compatible with that of the Latins, was forced to resign, and was replaced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Gregory_II_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Gregory II of Constantinople">Patriarch Gregory II of Constantinople</a>, who was strongly of the opposite opinion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulgakov2004104_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulgakov2004104-166">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lyons II did not require those Christians to change the recitation of the creed in their liturgy.</p>
<p>Lyons II stated "that the Holy Spirit proceeds eternally from the Father and the Son, not as from two principles, but one, not from two spirations but by only one," is "the unchangeable and true doctrine of the orthodox Fathers and Doctors, both Latin and Greek."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._850_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._850-158">[146]</a></sup> So, it "condemn[ed] and disapprove[d of] those who [ ] deny that the Holy Spirit proceeds eternally from Father and Son or who [ ] assert that the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Father and the Son as from two principles, not from one."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003_39-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._850_158-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._850-158">[146]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palaio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2502" data-file-width="1818" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Palaio.jpg/220px-Palaio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Palaio.jpg/330px-Palaio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Palaio.jpg/440px-Palaio.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Palaio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John VIII Palaiologos<br/>
by <a href="/wiki/Benozzo_Gozzoli" title="Benozzo Gozzoli">Benozzo Gozzoli</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another attempt at reunion was made at the 15th century <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Florence" title="Council of Florence">Council of Florence</a>, to which Emperor <a href="/wiki/John_VIII_Palaiologos" title="John VIII Palaiologos">John VIII Palaiologos</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_Joseph_II_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch Joseph II of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarch Joseph II of Constantinople</a>, and other bishops from the East had gone in the hope of getting Western military aid against the looming <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. Thirteen public sessions held in <a href="/wiki/Ferrara" title="Ferrara">Ferrara</a> from 8 October to 13 December 1438 the <i>Filioque</i> question was debated without agreement. The Greeks held that any addition whatever, even if doctrinally correct, to the Creed had been forbidden by Ephesus I, while the Latins claimed that this prohibition concerned meaning, not words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Florence.2C_Council_of.22_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Florence.2C_Council_of.22-167">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the Council of Florence in 1439, accord continued to be elusive, until the argument prevailed among the Greeks themselves that, though the Greek and the Latin saints expressed their faith differently, they were in agreement substantially, since saints cannot err in faith; and by 8 June the Greeks accepted the Latin statement of doctrine. Joseph II died on 10 June. A statement on the <i>Filioque</i> question was included in the <i>Laetentur Caeli</i> decree of union, which was signed on 5 July 1439 and promulgated the next day – Mark of Ephesus was the only bishop not to sign the agreement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Florence.2C_Council_of.22_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Florence.2C_Council_of.22-167">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Eastern Church refused to consider the agreement reached at Florence binding,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="How does something that is agreed to by all representative bishops, except one, change into a lack of consensus? (November 2015)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> since the death of Joseph II had for the moment left it without a Patriarch of Constantinople. There was strong opposition to the agreement in the East, and when in 1453, 14 years after the agreement, the promised military aid from the West still had not arrived and <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Constantinople fell</a> to the Turks, neither Eastern Christians nor their new rulers wished union between them and the West.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Councils_of_Jerusalem.2C_1583_and_1672_AD">Councils of Jerusalem, 1583 and 1672 AD</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Councils of Jerusalem, 1583 and 1672 AD">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Synod of Jerusalem (1583) condemned those who do not believe the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Father alone in essence, and from Father and Son in time. In addition, this synod re-affirmed adherence to the decisions of Nicaea I. The <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Jerusalem_(1672)" title="Synod of Jerusalem (1672)">Synod of Jerusalem (1672)</a> similarly re-affirmed procession of the Holy Spirit from the Father alone.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reformation">Reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although the Protestant Reformation challenged a number of church doctrines, they accepted the <i>Filioque</i> without reservation. However, they did not have a polemical insistence on the Western view of the Trinity. In the second half of the 16th century, Lutheran scholars from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_T%C3%BCbingen" title="University of Tübingen">University of Tübingen</a> initiated a dialogue with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Jeremias_II_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Jeremias II of Constantinople">Patriarch Jeremias II of Constantinople</a>. The Tübingen Lutherans defended the <i>Filioque</i> arguing that, without it, "the doctrine of the Trinity would lose its epistemological justification in the history of revelation." In the centuries that followed, the <i>Filioque</i> was considered by Protestant theologians to be a key component of the doctrine of the Trinity, although it was never elevated to being a pillar of Protestant theology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[156]</a></sup> Zizioulas characterize Protestants as finding themselves "in the same confusion as those fourth century theologians who were unable to distinguish between the two sorts of procession, 'proceeding from' and 'sent by'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[157]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Present_position_of_various_churches">Present position of various churches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Present position of various churches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Catholicism">Roman Catholicism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Roman Catholicism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Roman Catholic Church holds, as a truth dogmatically defined since as far back as Leo I in 447, who followed a <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Alexandria" title="Church of Alexandria">Alexandrian</a> tradition, that the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Father and the Son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeoI447_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-LeoI447-85">[79]</a></sup> It rejects the notion that the Holy Spirit proceeds jointly and equally from two principles (Father and Son) and teaches dogmatically that "the Holy Spirit proceeds eternally from the Father and the Son, not as from two principles but as from one single principle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._850_158-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._850-158">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup> It holds that the Father, as the "principle without principle", is the first origin of the Spirit, but also that he, as Father of the only Son, is with the Son the single principle from which the Spirit proceeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCC248_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-CCC248-110">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>It also holds that the procession of the Holy Spirit can be expressed as "from the Father through the Son". The agreement that brought about the 1595 <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Brest" title="Union of Brest">Union of Brest</a> expressly declared that those entering full communion with Rome "should remain with that which was handed down to (them) in the Holy Scriptures, in the Gospel, and in the writings of the holy Greek Doctors, that is, that the Holy Spirit proceeds, not from two sources and not by a double procession, but from one origin, from the Father through the Son."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCC248_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-CCC248-110">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brest_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brest-161">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Roman Catholic Church recognizes that the Creed, as confessed at the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Constantinople" title="First Council of Constantinople">First Council of Constantinople</a>, did not add "and the Son", when it spoke of the Holy Spirit as proceeding from the Father, and that this addition was admitted to the Latin liturgy between the 8th and 11th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCC247_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-CCC247-51">[50]</a></sup> When quoting the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr>, as in the 2000 document <i><a href="/wiki/Dominus_Iesus" title="Dominus Iesus">Dominus Iesus</a></i>, it does not include <i>Filioque</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[158]</a></sup> It views as complementary the Eastern-tradition expression "who proceeds from the Father" (profession of which it sees as affirming that he comes from the Father through the Son) and the Western-tradition expression "who proceeds from the Father and the Son", with the Eastern tradition expressing firstly the Father's character as first origin of the Spirit, and the Western tradition giving expression firstly to the consubstantial communion between Father and Son; and it believes that, provided this legitimate complementarity does not become rigid, it does not affect the identity of faith in the reality of the same mystery confessed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCC248_110-3"><a href="#cite_note-CCC248-110">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>The monarchy of the Father is a doctrine upheld not only by those who, like Photius, speak of a procession from the Father alone. It is also asserted by theologians who speak of a procession from the Father through the Son or from the Father and the Son. Examples cited by Siecienski include <a href="/wiki/Basilios_Bessarion" title="Basilios Bessarion">Basilios Bessarion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010163_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010163-171">[159]</a></sup> Maximus the Confessor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201081_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201081-172">[160]</a></sup> Bonaventure,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010127_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010127-173">[161]</a></sup> and the Council of Worms (868),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010105_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010105-174">[162]</a></sup> The same remark is made by <a href="/wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Moltmann" title="Jürgen Moltmann">Jürgen Moltmann</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[m]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_Council_for_Promoting_Christian_Unity" title="Pontifical Council for Promoting Christian Unity">Pontifical Council for Promoting Christian Unity</a> (PCPCU) also stated that not only the Eastern tradition, but also the Latin <i>Filioque</i> tradition "recognize that the 'Monarchy of the Father' implies that the Father is the sole Trinitarian Cause (<span lang="grc">αἰτία</span>) or Principle (<i><span lang="la">principium</span></i>) of the Son and of the Holy Spirit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Roman Catholic Church recognizes that, in the Greek language, the term used in the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> (<span lang="grc">ἐκπορευόμενον</span>, "proceeding") to signify the proceeding of the Holy Spirit cannot appropriately be used with regard to the Son, but only with regard to the Father, a difficulty that does not exist in other languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup> For this reason, even in the liturgy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_Rite" title="Latin Rite">Latin Rite</a> Catholics, it does not add the phrase corresponding to <i>Filioque</i> (<span lang="grc">καὶ τοῦ Υἱοῦ</span>) to the Greek language text of the Creed containing the word <span lang="grc">ἐκπορευόμενον</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup> Even in languages other than Greek, it encourages <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic_Churches" title="Eastern Catholic Churches">Eastern Catholic Churches</a> that in the past incorporated <i>Filioque</i> into their recitation of the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> to omit it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Younan2015_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Younan2015-177">[164]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Anglicanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The 1978 and 1988 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lambeth_Conferences" title="Lambeth Conferences">Lambeth Conferences</a> advised the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a> to omit printing the <i>Filioque</i> in <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELambeth_Conference1978res._35.3Lambeth_Conference1988res._6.5_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELambeth_Conference1978res._35.3Lambeth_Conference1988res._6.5-178">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1993, a joint meeting of the Anglican Primates and Anglican Consultative Council, passed a resolution urging Anglican churches to comply with the request to print the liturgical <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> without the <i>Filioque</i> clause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEACC1993res._19_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEACC1993res._19-179">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>The recommendation was not specifically renewed in the 1998 and 2008 Lambeth Conferences and has not been implemented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1985 the General Convention of The Episcopal Church (USA) recommended that the <i>Filioque</i> clause should be removed from the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr>, if this were endorsed by the 1988 Lambeth Council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[168]</a></sup> Accordingly, at its 1994 General Convention, the Episcopal Church reaffirmed its intention to remove the <i>Filioque</i> clause from the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> in the next revision of its Book of Common Prayer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[169]</a></sup> The Episcopal Book of Common Prayer was last revised in 1979, and has not been revised since the resolution.</p>
<p>The Scottish Episcopal Church no longer prints the <i>Filioque</i> clause in its modern language liturgies.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestantism">Protestantism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Protestantism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Among 20th century Protestant theologians, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Barth" title="Karl Barth">Karl Barth</a> was perhaps the staunchest defender of the <i>Filioque</i> doctrine. Barth was harshly critical of the ecumenical movement which advocated dropping the <i>Filioque</i> in order to facilitate reunification of the Christian churches. Barth's vigorous defense of the <i>Filioque</i> ran counter to the stance of many Protestant theologians of the latter half of the 20th century who favored abandoning the use of the <i>Filioque</i> in the liturgy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEECT2005.22Filioque.22_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEECT2005.22Filioque.22-183">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuretzki200912_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuretzki200912-184">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Moravian_Church" title="Moravian Church">Moravian Church</a>, a Protestant church with some roots in Eastern Orthodoxy, have never used the <i>Filioque</i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_Orthodoxy">Eastern Orthodoxy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Eastern Orthodoxy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_teaching_regarding_the_Filioque" title="Eastern Orthodox teaching regarding the Filioque">Eastern Orthodox teaching regarding the Filioque</a></div>
<p>The Eastern Orthodox interpretation is that the Holy Spirit originates, has his cause for existence or being (manner of existence) from the Father alone as "One God, One Father",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fone-god-one-father_One_God.2C_One_Father.5D.22_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fone-god-one-father_One_God.2C_One_Father.5D.22-185">[172]</a></sup> Lossky insisted that any notion of a double procession of the Holy Spirit from both the Father and the Son was incompatible with Orthodox theology. For Lossky, this incompatibility was so fundamental that "[W]hether we like it or not, the question of the procession of the Holy Spirit has been the sole <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogmatic</a> grounds of the separation of East and West."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaDue200363_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaDue200363-186">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELossky2003163_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELossky2003163-187">[174]</a></sup> Orthodox scholars who share Lossky's view include <a href="/wiki/Dumitru_St%C4%83niloae" title="Dumitru Stăniloae">Dumitru Stăniloae</a>, Romanides, <a href="/wiki/Christos_Yannaras" title="Christos Yannaras">Christos Yannaras</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[175]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The names Lossky Stăniloae Romanides Yannaras do not appear together in LaDues Book, but they doappear together on p15 of Orthodox Constructions of the West (November 2015)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Pomazansky" title="Michael Pomazansky">Michael Pomazansky</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Bulgakov" title="Sergei Bulgakov">Sergei Bulgakov</a>, however, was of the opinion that the <i>Filioque</i> did not represent an insurmountable obstacle to reunion of the Eastern Orthodox and Roman Catholic churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaDue200363_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaDue200363-186">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>In this view, the Son and the Spirit are both one with God and in no way separated from Him. Thus, the Divine Unity consists of the Father, with His Son and His Spirit distinct from Himself and yet perfectly united together in Him,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fone-god-one-father_One_God.2C_One_Father.5D.22_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fone-god-one-father_One_God.2C_One_Father.5D.22-185">[172]</a></sup> and the <i>Filioque</i> confuses the theology as it was defined at the councils at both Nicene and Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFarrell198775.E2.80.9376_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFarrell198775.E2.80.9376-189">[176]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Views_of_Eastern_Orthodox_saints">Views of Eastern Orthodox saints</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Views of Eastern Orthodox saints">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Maximus_the_Confessor" title="Maximus the Confessor">Maximus the Confessor</a> declared that it was wrong to condemn the Latins for speaking of the procession of the Holy Spirit from the Father and the Son, the addition of the <i>Filioque</i> to the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> was condemned as heretical by other saints of the Eastern Orthodox Church, including <a href="/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Photios I of Constantinople">Photios I of Constantinople</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Palamas" title="Gregory Palamas">Gregory Palamas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_of_Ephesus" title="Mark of Ephesus">Mark of Ephesus</a>, sometimes referred to as the Three Pillars of Orthodoxy. However, the statement 'The Holy Spirit proceeds from the Father and the Son' can be understood in an orthodox sense if it is clear from the context that 'procession from the Son' refers to the sending forth of the Spirit <i>in time</i>, not to an eternal, double procession within the Trinity Itself which gives the Holy Spirit existence or being. Hence, in Eastern Orthodox thought, Maximus the Confessor justified the Western use of the <i>Filioque</i> in a context other than that of the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPomazansky1984.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.intratext.com.2FIXT.2FENG0824.2F_P14.HTM_.22On_the_procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22.5D_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPomazansky1984.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.intratext.com.2FIXT.2FENG0824.2F_P14.HTM_.22On_the_procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22.5D-190">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[n]</a></sup> and "defended [the <i>Filioque</i>] as a legitimate variation of the Eastern formula that the Spirit proceeds from the Father <i>through</i> the Son."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDulles199532.2C_40_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDulles199532.2C_40-87">[80]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[...] it is said not that [the Holy Spirit] has existence from the Son or through the Son, but rather that [the Holy Spirit] proceeds from the Father and has the same nature as the Son, is in fact the Spirit of the Son as being One in Essence with Him.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodoret_of_Cyrus" title="Theodoret of Cyrus">Theodoret of Cyrus</a>, <i>On the Third Ecumenical Council</i><span style="white-space:nowrap"> </span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPomazansky1984.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.intratext.com.2FIXT.2FENG0824.2F_P14.HTM_.22On_the_procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22.5D_190-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPomazansky1984.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.intratext.com.2FIXT.2FENG0824.2F_P14.HTM_.22On_the_procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22.5D-190">[177]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Hierotheos_(Vlachos)_of_Nafpaktos" title="Metropolitan Hierotheos (Vlachos) of Nafpaktos">Metropolitan Hierotheos (Vlachos) of Nafpaktos</a>, an Eastern Orthodox tradition is that <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Nyssa" title="Gregory of Nyssa">Gregory of Nyssa</a> composed the section of the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> referring to the Holy Spirit adopted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Ecumenical_Council" title="Second Ecumenical Council">Second Ecumenical Council</a> at Constantinople in 381.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[o]</a></sup> Siecienski doubts that Gregory of Nyssa would have endorsed the addition of the <i>Filioque</i>, as later understood in the West, into the Creed, notwithstanding that Gregory of Nyssa reasoned "there is an eternal, and not simply economic, relationship of the Spirit to the Son."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201043.E2.80.9345_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201043.E2.80.9345-194">[179]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_Orthodox_view_of_Roman_Catholic_theology">Eastern Orthodox view of Roman Catholic theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Eastern Orthodox view of Roman Catholic theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Eastern Orthodox theologians (e.g., Pomazansky) say that the Nicene Creed as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbol_of_Faith" title="Symbol of Faith">Symbol of Faith</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogma</a>, is to address and define church theology specifically the Orthodox Trinitarian understanding of God. In the hypostases of God as correctly expressed against the teachings considered outside the church. The Father <a href="/wiki/Hypostatic_union" title="Hypostatic union">hypostasis</a> of the Nicene Creed is the origin of all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[p]</a></sup> Eastern Orthodox theologians have stated that New Testament passages (often quoted by the Latins) speak of the economy rather than the ontology of the Holy Spirit, and that in order to resolve this conflict Western theologians made further doctrinal changes, including declaring all persons of the Trinity to originate in the essence of God (the heresy of <a href="/wiki/Sabellianism" title="Sabellianism">Sabellianism</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELossky199748.E2.80.9357_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELossky199748.E2.80.9357-197">[181]</a></sup> Eastern Orthodox theologians see this as teaching of philosophical speculation rather than from <a href="/wiki/Theoria#Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Theoria">actual experience of God via <i>theoria</i></a>.</p>
<p>The Father is the eternal, infinite and uncreated reality, that the Christ and the Holy Spirit are also eternal, infinite and uncreated, in that their origin is not in the <i><a href="/wiki/Ousia" title="Ousia">ousia</a></i> of God, but that their origin is in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypostasis_(philosophy)" title="Hypostasis (philosophy)">hypostasis</a> of God called the Father. The double procession of the Holy Spirit bears some resemblance<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[q]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[r]</a></sup></p>
<p>The following are some Roman Catholic dogmatic declarations of the <i>Filioque</i> which are in contention with Eastern Orthodoxy:</p>
<ol>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Council_of_the_Lateran" title="Fourth Council of the Lateran">Fourth Council of the Lateran</a> (1215): "The Father is from no one, the Son from the Father only, and the Holy Spirit equally from both."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._800_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._800-201">[183]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Lyon" title="Second Council of Lyon">Second Council of Lyon</a>, session 2 (1274): "[ ] the Holy Spirit proceeds eternally from Father and Son, not as from two principles, but as from one, not by two spirations, but by one only."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._850_158-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._850-158">[146]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Council of Florence, session 6 (1439) : "We declare that when holy doctors and fathers say that the holy Spirit proceeds from the Father through the Son, this bears the sense that thereby also the Son should be signified, according to the Greeks indeed as cause, and according to the Latins as principle of the subsistence of the Holy Spirit, just like the Father."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[184]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Council of Florence, session 8 in <i><a href="/wiki/Laetentur_Caeli" title="Laetentur Caeli">Laetentur Caeli</a></i> (1439), on union with the Greeks: "The Holy Spirit is eternally from Father and Son; He has his nature and subsistence at once (simul) from the Father and the Son. He proceeds eternally from both as from one principle and through one spiration. ... And, since the Father has through generation given to the only-begotten Son everything that belongs to the Father, except being Father, the Son has also eternally from the Father, from whom he is eternally born, that the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Son."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012nn.1300.E2.80.931301_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012nn.1300.E2.80.931301-203">[185]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Council of Florence, session 11 (1442), in <i>Cantate Domino</i>, on union with the Copts and Ethiopians: "Father, Son and holy Spirit; one in essence, three in persons; unbegotten Father, Son begotten from the Father, holy Spirit proceeding from the Father and the Son; [...] the holy Spirit alone proceeds at once from the Father and the Son. [...] Whatever the holy Spirit is or has, he has from the Father together with the Son. But the Father and the Son are not two principles of the holy Spirit, but one principle, just as the Father and the Son and the holy Spirit are not three principles of creation but one principle."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012nn.1330.E2.80.931331_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012nn.1330.E2.80.931331-204">[186]</a></sup></li>
<li>In particular the condemnation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003_39-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003-39">[38]</a></sup> made at the Second Council of Lyons, session 2 (1274), of those "who [ ] deny that the Holy Spirit proceeds eternally from the Father and the Son or who [ ] assert that the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Father and the Son as from two principles, not from one."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._850_158-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._850-158">[146]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>In the judgment of these Orthodox,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (December 2015)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> the Roman Catholic Church is in fact teaching as a matter of Roman Catholic dogma that the Holy Spirit derives his origin and being (equally) from both the Father and the Son, making the <i>Filioque</i> a double procession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[s]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKulakov2007177_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKulakov2007177-207">[188]</a></sup><sup class="noprint selfreference">[<i><a href="/wiki/Talk:Filioque#Lossky_in_Kulakov" title="Talk:Filioque">discuss</a></i>]</sup> This being the very thing that Maximus the Confessor was stating in his work from the 7th century that would be wrong and that the West was not doing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romanides1_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Romanides1-134">[123]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (November 2011)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>They<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (December 2015)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> perceive the West as teaching through more than one type of theological <i>Filioque</i> a different origin and cause of the Holy Spirit. That through the dogmatic Roman Catholic <i>Filioque</i> the Holy Spirit is subordinate to the Father and the Son and not a free and independent and equal to the Father, hypostasis that receives his uncreatedness from the origin of all things, the Father hypostasis. Trinity expresses the idea of message, messenger and revealer, or mind, word and meaning. Eastern Orthodox Christians believe in one God the Father, whose person is uncaused and unoriginate, who, because He is love and communion, always exists with His Word and Spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[t]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[u]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_Orthodox_theology">Eastern Orthodox theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Eastern Orthodox theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Eastern Orthodox Christianity theology starts with the Father hypostasis, not the essence of God, since the Father is the God of the Old Testament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fone-god-one-father_One_God.2C_One_Father.5D.22_185-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fone-god-one-father_One_God.2C_One_Father.5D.22-185">[172]</a></sup> The Father is the origin of all things and this is the basis and starting point of the Orthodox trinitarian teaching of one God in Father, one God, of the essence of the Father (as the uncreated comes from the Father as this is what the Father is).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fone-god-one-father_One_God.2C_One_Father.5D.22_185-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fone-god-one-father_One_God.2C_One_Father.5D.22-185">[172]</a></sup> In Eastern Orthodox theology, God's uncreatedness or being or essence in Greek is called <i><a href="/wiki/Ousia" title="Ousia">ousia</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPapanikolaou2011_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPapanikolaou2011-211">[190]</a></sup> Jesus Christ is the Son (God Man) of the uncreated Father (God). The Holy Spirit is the Spirit of the uncreated Father (God).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fthe-three-divine-persons_The_three_Divine_Persons.5D.22_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fthe-three-divine-persons_The_three_Divine_Persons.5D.22-212">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">The activity and actuality of the Trinity in creation are called God's energies as God as creator is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tabor_light" title="Tabor light">light</a> and this uncreated light (energy) is the basis from which all things derive their existence.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> God has existences (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypostasis_(philosophy)" title="Hypostasis (philosophy)">hypostases</a>) of being; this concept is translated as the word "person" in the West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fthe-three-divine-persons_The_three_Divine_Persons.5D.22_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fthe-three-divine-persons_The_three_Divine_Persons.5D.22-212">[191]</a></sup> Each hypostasis of God is a specific and unique existence of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fthe-three-divine-persons_The_three_Divine_Persons.5D.22_212-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fthe-three-divine-persons_The_three_Divine_Persons.5D.22-212">[191]</a></sup> Each has the same essence (coming from the origin, without origin, Father (God) they are uncreated).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fthe-three-divine-persons_The_three_Divine_Persons.5D.22_212-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fthe-three-divine-persons_The_three_Divine_Persons.5D.22-212">[191]</a></sup> Each specific quality that constitutes an hypostasis of God, is non-reductionist and not shared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fthe-three-divine-persons_The_three_Divine_Persons.5D.22_212-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkon.d..28a.29.22.5Bhttp:.2F.2Foca.org.2Forthodoxy.2Fthe-orthodox-faith.2Fdoctrine.2Fthe-holy-trinity.2Fthe-three-divine-persons_The_three_Divine_Persons.5D.22-212">[191]</a></sup> The issue of ontology or being of the Holy Spirit is also complicated by the <i>Filioque</i> in that the <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christology</a> and uniqueness of the hypostasis of Jesus Christ would factor into the manifestation of the Holy Spirit. In that Jesus is both God and Man, which fundamentally changes the hypostasis or being of the Holy Spirit, as Christ would be giving to the Holy Spirit an origin or being that was both God the Father (Uncreated) and Man (createdness).</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Immanence" title="Immanence">immanence</a> of the Trinity that was defined in the finalized Nicene Creed. The economy of God, as God expresses himself in reality (his energies) was not what the Creed addressed directly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuckin2011b170.E2.80.93171_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuckin2011b170.E2.80.93171-213">[192]</a></sup> The specifics of God's interrelationships of his existences, are not defined within the Nicene Creed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuckin2011b170.E2.80.93171_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuckin2011b170.E2.80.93171-213">[192]</a></sup> The attempt to use the Creed to explain God's energies by reducing God existences to mere energies (actualities, activities, potentials) could be perceived as the heresy of semi-<a href="/wiki/Sabellianism" title="Sabellianism">Sabellianism</a> by advocates of <a href="/wiki/Personalism" title="Personalism">Personalism</a>, according to Meyendorff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff1996178_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff1996178-214">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.intratext.com.2FIXT.2FENG0804.2F_P12.HTM_God_in_Trinity.5D_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.intratext.com.2FIXT.2FENG0804.2F_P12.HTM_God_in_Trinity.5D-215">[194]</a></sup> Eastern Orthodox theologians have complained about this problem in the Roman Catholic dogmatic teaching of <i><a href="/wiki/Actus_purus" title="Actus purus">actus purus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff1986.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.myriobiblos.gr.2Ftexts.2Fenglish.2FMeyendorff_13.html_.C2.A73.5D_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff1986.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.myriobiblos.gr.2Ftexts.2Fenglish.2FMeyendorff_13.html_.C2.A73.5D-216">[195]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_theology">Modern theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Modern theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Modern Orthodox theological scholarship is split, according to William La Due, between a group of scholars that hold to a "strict traditionalism going back to Photius" and other scholars "not so adamantly opposed to the <i>filioque</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaDue200363_186-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaDue200363-186">[173]</a></sup> The "strict traditionalist" camp is exemplified by the stance of Lossky who insisted that any notion of a double procession of the Holy Spirit from both the Father and the Son was incompatible with Orthodox theology. For Lossky, this incompatibility was so fundamental that, "whether we like it or not, the question of the procession of the Holy Spirit has been the sole <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogmatic</a> grounds of the separation of East and West."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaDue200363_186-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaDue200363-186">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELossky2003163_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELossky2003163-187">[174]</a></sup> Bulgakov, however, was of the opinion that the <i>Filioque</i> did not represent an insurmountable obstacle to reunion of the Eastern Orthodox and Roman Catholic churches,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaDue200363_186-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaDue200363-186">[173]</a></sup> an opinion shared by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Vasily_Bolotov&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Vasily Bolotov (page does not exist)">Vasily Bolotov</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> (<a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%91%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%B2,_%D0%92%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%B8%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%92%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%B8%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%87" title="ru:Болотов, Василий Васильевич">ru</a>)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBalthasar2005209_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBalthasar2005209-217">[196]</a></sup></p>
<p>Not all Orthodox theologians share the view taken by Lossky, Stăniloae, Romanides and Pomazansky, who condemn the <i>Filioque</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LutheranOrthodox1998_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-LutheranOrthodox1998-218">[197]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kallistos_Ware" title="Kallistos Ware">Kallistos Ware</a> considers this the "rigorist" position within the Orthodox Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare2006209_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare2006209-219">[198]</a></sup> Ware states that a more "liberal" position on this issue "was the view of the Greeks who signed the act of union at Florence. It is a view also held by many Orthodox at the present time". He writes that "according to the 'liberal' view, the Greek and the Latin doctrines on the procession of the Holy Spirit may both alike be regarded as theologically defensible. The Greeks affirm that the Spirit proceeds from the Father <i>through</i> the Son, the Latins that He proceeds from the Father <i>and</i> from the Son; but when applied to the relationship between Son and Spirit, these two prepositions 'through' and 'from' amount to the same thing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare2006208_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare2006208-220">[199]</a></sup> The <i>Encyclopedia of Christian Theology</i> lists Bolotov,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEECT2005.22Filioque.22_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEECT2005.22Filioque.22-183">[170]</a></sup> Paul Evdokimov, I. Voronov and Bulgakov as seeing the <i>Filioque</i> as a permissible theological opinion or "theologoumenon."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEECT2005.22Filioque.22_183-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEECT2005.22Filioque.22-183">[170]</a></sup> Bolotov defined theologoumena as theological opinions "of those who for every catholic are more than just theologians: they are the theological opinions of the holy fathers of the one undivided church", opinions that Bolotov rated highly but that he sharply distinguished from dogmas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAOJDC1984n._45_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAOJDC1984n._45-221">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bulgakov wrote, in <i>The Comforter</i>, that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is a difference of theological opinions which was dogmatized prematurely and erroneously. There is no dogma of the relation of the Holy Spirit to the Son and therefore particular opinions on this subject are not heresies but merely dogmatic hypotheses, which have been transformed into heresies by the schismatic spirit that has established itself in the Church and that eagerly exploits all sorts of liturgical and even cultural differences"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulgakov2004148_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulgakov2004148-222">[201]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Karl_Barth" title="Karl Barth">Karl Barth</a> considered that the view prevailing in Eastern Orthodoxy was that of Bolotov, who pointed out that the Creed does not deny the <i>Filioque</i> and who concluded that the question had not caused the division and could not constitute an absolute obstacle to intercommunion between the Eastern Orthodox and the <a href="/wiki/Old_Catholic_Church" title="Old Catholic Church">Old Catholic Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[202]</a></sup> David Guretzki wrote, in 2009, that Bolotov's view is becoming more prevalent among Orthodox theologians; and he quotes Orthodox theologian Theodore Stylianopoulos as arguing that "the theological use of the filioque in the West against Arian subordinationism is fully valid according to the theological criteria of the Eastern tradition".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuretzki2009119_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuretzki2009119-224">[203]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Yves_Congar" title="Yves Congar">Yves Congar</a> stated in 1954 that, "the greater number of the Orthodox say that the <i>Filioque</i> is not a heresy or even a dogmatic error but an admissible theological opinion, a 'theologoumenon';" and he cited 12th century bishop <a href="/wiki/Nicetas_of_Nicomedia" title="Nicetas of Nicomedia">Nicetas of Nicomedia</a>; 19th century philosopher <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Solovyov_(philosopher)" title="Vladimir Solovyov (philosopher)">Vladimir Solovyov</a>; and 20th century writers Bolotov, Florovsky, and Bulgakov.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECongar1959147.E2.80.93148_n._28_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECongar1959147.E2.80.93148_n._28-225">[204]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_of_the_East">Church of the East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Church of the East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Two of the present-day churches derived from the Church of the East, the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Church_of_the_East" title="Ancient Church of the East">Ancient Church of the East</a> do not use "and the Son" when reciting the Nicene Creed. A third, the <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">Chaldean Catholic Church</a>, a <i><a href="/wiki/Sui_iuris" title="Sui iuris">sui iuris</a></i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic_Church" title="Eastern Catholic Church">Eastern Catholic Church</a>, has recently, at the request of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a>, removed "and the Son" from its version of the Nicene Creed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Younan2015_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Younan2015-177">[164]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_theological_perspectives">Recent theological perspectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Recent theological perspectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linguistic_issues">Linguistic issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Linguistic issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Perichoresis" title="Perichoresis">Perichoresis</a></div>
<p>Ware suggests that the problem is of semantics rather than of basic doctrinal differences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LutheranOrthodox1998_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-LutheranOrthodox1998-218">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[205]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="The work is a translation into English and there are already a few works by Ware that may contain the equivalent (November 2015)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/English_Language_Liturgical_Consultation" title="English Language Liturgical Consultation">English Language Liturgical Consultation</a> commented that "those who strongly favor retention of the <i>Filioque</i> are often thinking of the Trinity as revealed and active in human affairs, whereas the original Greek text is concerned about relationships within the Godhead itself. As with many historic disputes, the two parties may not be discussing the same thing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[206]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1995, the <abbr title="Pontifical Council for Promoting Christian Unity">PCPCU</abbr> pointed out an important difference in meaning between the Greek verb <span lang="grc">ἐκπορεύεσθαι</span> and the Latin verb <i><span lang="la">procedere</span></i>, both of which are commonly translated as "proceed". It stated that the Greek verb <span lang="grc">ἐκπορεύεσθαι</span> indicates that the Spirit "takes his origin from the Father ... in a principal, proper and immediate manner", while the Latin verb, which corresponds rather to the verb <span lang="grc">προϊέναι</span> in Greek, can be applied to proceeding even from a mediate channel. Therefore, <span lang="grc">ἐκπορευόμενον</span> ("who proceeds"), used in the <abbr title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">NCC</abbr> to signify the proceeding of the Holy Spirit, cannot be appropriately used in the Greek language with regard to the Son, but only with regard to the Father, a difficulty that does not exist in Latin and other languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Metropolitan <a href="/wiki/John_Zizioulas" title="John Zizioulas">John Zizioulas</a>, while maintaining the explicit Orthodox position of the Father as the single origin and source of the Holy Spirit, declared that <a href="#CITEREFPCPCU1995">PCPCU (1995)</a> shows positive signs of reconciliation. Zizioulas states: "Closely related to the question of the single cause is the problem of the exact meaning of the Son's involvement in the procession of the Spirit. <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Nyssa" title="Gregory of Nyssa">Gregory of Nyssa</a> explicitly admits a 'mediating' role of the Son in the procession of the Spirit from the Father. Is this role to be expressed with the help of the preposition <span lang="grc">δία</span> (through) the Son (<span lang="grc">εκ Πατρός δι'Υιού</span>), as Maximus and other Patristic sources seem to suggest?" Zizioulas continues: "The Vatican statement notes that this is 'the basis that must serve for the continuation of the current theological dialogue between Catholic and Orthodox'. I would agree with this, adding that the discussion should take place in the light of the 'single cause' principle to which I have just referred." Zizioulas adds that this "constitutes an encouraging attempt to clarify the basic aspects of the 'Filioque' problem and show that a rapprochement between West and East on this matter is eventually possible".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZizioulas1996_195-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZizioulas1996-195">[180]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Some_Orthodox_reconsideration_of_the_Filioque">Some Orthodox reconsideration of the <i>Filioque</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Some Orthodox reconsideration of the Filioque">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several Orthodox theologians have considered the <i>Filioque</i> anew, with a view to reconciliation of East and West. In 1898, Russian theologian Boris Bolotov asserted in 1898 that the <i>Filioque</i>, like Photios's "from the Father <i>alone</i>", was a permissible theological opinion (a theologoumenon, not a dogma) that cannot be an absolute impediment to reestablishment of communion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010190.E2.80.93191_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010190.E2.80.93191-228">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEECT2005.22Filioque.22_183-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEECT2005.22Filioque.22-183">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[208]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Bolotov's thesis was supported by Orthodox theologians Bulgakov, Paul Evdokimov and I. Voronov, but was rejected by Lossky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEECT2005.22Filioque.22_183-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEECT2005.22Filioque.22-183">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1986, Theodore Stylianopoulos provided an extensive, scholarly overview of the contemporary discussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStylianopoulous1984_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStylianopoulous1984-230">[209]</a></sup> Ware said, that he had changed his mind and had concluded that "the problem is more in the area of semantics and different emphases than in any basic doctrinal differences": "the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Father alone" and "the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Father and the Son" may <i>both</i> have orthodox meanings if the words translated "proceeds" actually have different meanings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[210]</a></sup> For some Orthodox<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (November 2011)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup>, then, the <i>Filioque</i>, while still a matter of conflict, would not impede full communion of the Roman Catholic and Orthodox Churches if other issues were resolved.
