But 19th century Russian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavophile" title="Slavophile">Slavophile</a> theologian <a href="/wiki/Aleksey_Khomyakov" title="Aleksey Khomyakov">Aleksey Khomyakov</a> considered the <i>Filioque</i> as an expression of formalism, rationalism, pride and lack of love for other Christians,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Stay_on_topic" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles"><span title="The material near this tag may contain information that is not relevant to the article's main topic. (November 2015)">relevant?</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Filioque#Khomyakov_in_Lossky" title="Talk:Filioque">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lossky_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lossky-233">[v]</a></sup> and that it is in flagrant contravention of the words of Christ in the Gospel, has been specifically condemned by the Orthodox Church, and remains a fundamental heretical teaching which divides East and West.</p>
<p>Romanides too, while personally opposing the <i>Filioque</i> stated that Constantinople I was not ever interpreted "as a condemnation" of the doctrine "outside the Creed, since it did not teach that the Son is 'cause' or 'co-cause' of the existence of the Holy Spirit. This could not be added to the Creed where 'procession' means 'cause' of existence of the Holy Spirit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romanides1987_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Romanides1987-234">[212]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inclusion_in_the_Nicene_Creed">Inclusion in the Nicene Creed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Inclusion in the Nicene Creed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Eastern Orthodox Christians object that, even if the teaching of the <i>Filioque</i> can be defended, its medieval interpretation and unilateral interpolation into the Creed is anti-canonical and unacceptable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lossky_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lossky-233">[v]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEECT2005.22Filioque.22_183-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEECT2005.22Filioque.22-183">[170]</a></sup> "The Catholic Church acknowledges the conciliar, ecumenical, normative and irrevocable value, as expression of the one common faith of the Church and of all Christians, of the Symbol professed in Greek at Constantinople in 381 by the Second Ecumenical Council. No profession of faith peculiar to a particular liturgical tradition can contradict this expression of the faith taught and professed by the undivided Church."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup> The Catholic Church allows liturgical use of the <a href="/wiki/Apostles%27_Creed" title="Apostles' Creed">Apostles' Creed</a> as well of the Nicene Creed, and sees no essential difference between the recitation in the liturgy of a creed with orthodox additions and a profession of faith outside the liturgy such that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Tarasios_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Tarasios of Constantinople">Patriarch Tarasios of Constantinople</a>, who developed the Nicene Creed with an addition as follows: "the Holy Spirit, the Lord and giver of life, who proceeds from the Father <i>through the Son</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup> It sees the addition of "and the Son" in the context of the Latin <i><span lang="la">qui ex Patre procedit</span></i> (who proceeds from the Father) as an elucidation of the faith expressed by the Church Fathers, since the verb <i><span lang="la">procedere</span></i> signifies "the communication of the consubstantial divinity from the Father to the Son and from the Father, through and with the Son, to the Holy Spirit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most Oriental Orthodox churches have not added the <i>Filoque</i> to their creeds but the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolic Church</a> has added <a class="external text" href="http://www.armenianchurchlibrary.com/files/creed.pdf" rel="nofollow">elucidations</a> to the Nicene Creed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200938Nersessian201033_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200938Nersessian201033-235">[213]</a></sup> Another change made to the text of the Nicene Creed by both the Latins and the Greeks is to use the singular "I believe" in place of the plural "we believe", while all the Churches of <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodoxy" title="Oriental Orthodoxy">Oriental Orthodoxy</a>, not only the Armenian, but also the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[214]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[215]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malankara_Orthodox_Church" title="Malankara Orthodox Church">Malankara Orthodox Church</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[216]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_Orthodox_Church" title="Syrian Orthodox Church">Syrian Orthodox Church</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[217]</a></sup> have on the contrary preserved the "we believe" of the original text.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Focus_on_Saint_Maximus_as_a_point_of_mutual_agreement">Focus on Saint Maximus as a point of mutual agreement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Focus on Saint Maximus as a point of mutual agreement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Recently, theological debate about the <i>Filioque</i> has focused on the writings of Maximus the Confessor. Siecienski writes that, "Among the hundreds of figures involved in the filioque debates throughout the centuries, Maximus the Confessor enjoys a privileged position." During the lengthy proceedings at Ferrara-Florence, the Orthodox delegates presented a text from Maximus the Confessor that they felt could provide the key to resolving the theological differences between East and West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[218]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <abbr title="Pontifical Council for Promoting Christian Unity">PCPCU</abbr> states that, according to Maximus, the phrase "and from the Son" does not contradict the Holy Spirit's procession from the Father as first origin (ἐκπόρευσις), since it concerns only the Holy Spirit's coming (in the sense of the Latin word <i><span lang="la">processio</span></i> and Cyril of Alexandria's <span lang="grc">προϊέναι</span>) from the Son in a way that excludes any idea of <a href="/wiki/Subordinationism" title="Subordinationism">subordinationism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-11"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[w]</a></sup></p>
<p>Orthodox theologian and Metropolitan of Pergamon, <a href="/wiki/John_Zizioulas" title="John Zizioulas">John Zizioulas</a>, wrote that for Maximus the Confessor "the Filioque was not heretical because its intention was to denote not the <span lang="grc">ἐκπορεύεσθαι</span> (<span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration">ekporeuesthai</span>) but the <span lang="grc">προϊέναι</span> (<span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration">proienai</span>) of the Spirit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZizioulas1996_195-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZizioulas1996-195">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>Zizioulas also wrote that, "Maximus the Confessor insisted, however, in defence of the Roman use of the Filioque, the decisive thing in this defence lies precisely in the point that in using the Filioque the Romans do not imply a "cause" other than the Father. The notion of "cause" seems to be of special significance and importance in the Greek Patristic argument concerning the Filioque. If Roman Catholic theology would be ready to admit that the Son in no way constitutes a "cause" (aition) in the procession of the Spirit, this would bring the two traditions much closer to each other with regard to the Filioque."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZizioulas1996_195-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZizioulas1996-195">[180]</a></sup> This is precisely what Maximus said of the Roman view, that "they have shown that they have not made the Son the cause of the Spirit — they know in fact that the Father is the only cause of the Son and the Spirit, the one by begetting and the other by procession".</p>
<p>The <abbr title="Pontifical Council for Promoting Christian Unity">PCPCU</abbr> upholds the monarchy of the Father as the "sole Trinitarian Cause [aitia] or principle [principium] of the Son and the Holy Spirit." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-12"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup> While the Council of Florence proposed the equivalency of the two terms "cause" and "principle" and therefore implied that the Son is a cause (aitia) of the subsistence of the Holy Spirit, the <abbr title="Pontifical Council for Promoting Christian Unity">PCPCU</abbr> distinguishes "between what the Greeks mean by 'procession' in the sense of taking origin from, applicable only to the Holy Spirit relative to the Father (ek tou Patros ekporeuomenon), and what the Latins mean by 'procession' as the more common term applicable to both Son and Spirit (<span lang="la">ex Patre Filioque procedit</span>; ek tou Patros kai tou Huiou proion). This preserves the monarchy of the Father as the sole origin of the Holy Spirit while simultaneously allowing for an intratrinitarian relation between the Son and Holy Spirit that the document defines as 'signifying the communication of the consubstantial divinity from the Father to the Son and from the Father through and with the Son to the Holy Spirit'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[219]</a></sup></p>
<p>Roman Catholic theologian <a href="/wiki/Avery_Dulles" title="Avery Dulles">Avery Dulles</a> wrote that the Eastern fathers were aware of the currency of the <i>Filioque</i> in the West and did not generally regard it as heretical: Some, such as Maximus the Confessor, "defended it as a legitimate variation of the Eastern formula that the Spirit proceeds from the Father through the Son."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDulles199532.2C_40_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDulles199532.2C_40-87">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pomazansky and Romanides<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romanides1987_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-Romanides1987-234">[212]</a></sup> hold that Maximus' position does not defend the actual way the Roman Catholic Church justifies and teaches the <i>Filioque</i> as dogma for the whole church. While accepting as a legitimate and complementary expression of the same faith and reality the teaching that the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Father through the Son,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCC248_110-4"><a href="#cite_note-CCC248-110">[101]</a></sup> Maximus held strictly to the teaching of the Eastern Church that "the Father is the only cause of the Son and the Spirit"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[220]</a></sup> and wrote a special treatise about this dogma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romanides1987_234-2"><a href="#cite_note-Romanides1987-234">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPomazansky1984.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.intratext.com.2FIXT.2FENG0824.2F_P14.HTM_.22On_the_procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22.5D_190-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPomazansky1984.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.intratext.com.2FIXT.2FENG0824.2F_P14.HTM_.22On_the_procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22.5D-190">[177]</a></sup> And the Roman Catholic Church cites Maximus as in full accord with the teaching on the <i>Filioque</i> that it proposes for the whole Church as a dogma that is in harmony with the formula "from the Father through the Son",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-13"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup> for he explained that, by <i>ekporeusis</i>, "the Father is the sole cause of the Son and the Spirit", but that, by <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration">proienai</span>, the Greek verb corresponding to <i><span lang="la">procedere</span></i> (proceed) in Latin, the Spirit comes through the Son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995_52-14"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCPCU1995-52">[51]</a></sup> Later again at the Council of Florence in 1438, declared that the Greek formula "from the Father through the Son" was equivalent to the Latin "from the Father and the Son", not contradictory, and that those who used the two formulas "were aiming at the same meaning in different words".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERush1997168_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERush1997168-246">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKasper2004109_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKasper2004109-247">[224]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Per_filium"><i>Per filium</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Per filium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Recently some Orthodox theologians have proposed the substitution of the formula <i>a patre per filium / εκ του πατρός δια του υιού</i> (from the Father through the Son) instead of <i>a patre filioque</i> (from the Father and the Son).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Breck2001_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Breck2001-248">[225]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_attempts_at_reconciliation">Recent attempts at reconciliation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Recent attempts at reconciliation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Starting in the latter half of the nineteenth century, ecumenical efforts have gradually developed more nuanced understandings of the issues underlying the Filioque controversy and worked to remove them as an obstruction to Christian unity. Lossky insists that the <i>Filioque</i> is so fundamentally incompatible with Orthodox Christianity as to be the central issue dividing the two churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELossky2003163_187-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELossky2003163-187">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[x]</a></sup></p>
<p>Western churches have arrived at the position that, although the <i>Filioque</i> is doctrinally sound, the way that it was inserted into the Nicene Creed has created an unnecessary obstacle to ecumenical dialogue. Thus, without abandoning the <i>Filioque</i>, some Western churches have come to accept that it could be omitted from the Creed without violating any core theological principles. This accommodation on the part of Western Churches has the objective of allowing both East and West to once again to share a common understanding of the Creed as the traditional and fundamental statement of the Christian faith.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Catholic_Church">Old Catholic Church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Old Catholic Church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Immediately after the <a href="/wiki/Old_Catholic_Church" title="Old Catholic Church">Old Catholic Church</a> separated from the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> in 1871, its theologians initiated contact with the Orthodox Church. In 1874/75, representatives of the two churches held "union conferences" in <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a> with theologians of the Anglican Communion and the Lutheran Church in attendance in an unofficial capacity. The conferences discussed a number of issues including the filioque controversy. From the outset, Old Catholic theologians agreed with the Orthodox position that the <i>Filioque</i> had been introduced in the West in an unacceptably non-canonical way. It was at these Bonn conferences that the Old Catholics became the first Western church to omit the <i>Filioque</i> from the Nicene Creed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuretzki200911_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuretzki200911-250">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoltmann1993179.E2.80.93180_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoltmann1993179.E2.80.93180-251">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThomasWondra2002_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-ThomasWondra2002-252">[228]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anglican_Communion">Anglican Communion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Anglican Communion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lambeth_Conferences" title="Lambeth Conferences">Lambeth Conferences</a> (1888, 1978 and 1988) have recommended that the <i>Filioque</i> be dropped from the Nicene Creed by churches that belong to the Anglican Communion.</p>
<p>The 1930 Lambeth Conference initiated formal theological dialogue between representatives of the Anglican and Orthodox churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KennedyKennedy2008_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-KennedyKennedy2008-253">[229]</a></sup> In 1976, the Agreed Statement of the Anglican-Orthodox Joint Doctrinal Commission recommended that the <i>Filioque</i> should be omitted from the Creed because its inclusion had been effected without the authority of an Ecumenical Council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAOJDC1976nn._19.E2.80.9321_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAOJDC1976nn._19.E2.80.9321-254">[230]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1994, the General Convention of the Episcopal Church resolved that the <i>Filioque</i> should be deleted from the Nicene Creed in the next edition of the Prayer Book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArmentroutSlocum2005_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArmentroutSlocum2005-255">[231]</a></sup> The enthronement ceremonies of three recent archbishops of Canterbury (Robert Runcie, George Carey, Rowan Williams) included recitations of the Nicene Creed that omitted the <i>Filioque</i>; this has been considered to have been "a gesture of friendship toward Orthodox guests and their Communions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buchanan2006_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buchanan2006-256">[232]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Council_of_Churches">World Council of Churches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: World Council of Churches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1979, a study group of the <a href="/wiki/World_Council_of_Churches" title="World Council of Churches">World Council of Churches</a> examined the <i>Filioque</i> question and recommended that "the original form of the Creed, without the <i>Filioque</i>, should everywhere be recognized as the normative one and restored, so that the whole Christian people may be able ... to confess their common faith in the Holy Spirit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWCCFO1979_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWCCFO1979-5">[5]</a></sup> However, nearly a decade later, the WCC lamented that very few member churches had implemented this recommendation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuretzki200912_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuretzki200912-184">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Catholic">Roman Catholic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Roman Catholic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Popes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Paul_II" title="John Paul II">John Paul II</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benedict_XVI" title="Benedict XVI">Benedict XVI</a> have recited the Nicene Creed jointly with Patriarchs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Demetrius_I_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch Demetrius I of Constantinople">Demetrius I</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bartholomew_I" title="Bartholomew I">Bartholomew I</a> in Greek without the <i>Filioque</i> clause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003_39-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[233]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Joint_statement_of_Eastern_Orthodox_and_Roman_Catholic_theologians">Joint statement of Eastern Orthodox and Roman Catholic theologians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Joint statement of Eastern Orthodox and Roman Catholic theologians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>Filioque</i> was discussed at the 62nd meeting of the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Orthodox-Catholic_Theological_Consultation" title="North American Orthodox-Catholic Theological Consultation">North American Orthodox-Catholic Theological Consultation</a>, in 2002. In October 2003, the Consultation issued an agreed statement, <i>The Filioque: a Church-dividing issue?</i>, which provides an extensive review of Scripture, history, and theology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003_39-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003-39">[38]</a></sup> The recommendations include:</p>
<ol>
<li>That all involved in such dialogue expressly recognize the limitations of our ability to make definitive assertions about the inner life of God.</li>
<li>That, in the future, because of the progress in mutual understanding that has come about in recent decades, Orthodox and Catholics refrain from labeling as heretical the traditions of the other side on the subject of the procession of the Holy Spirit.</li>
<li>That Orthodox and Catholic theologians distinguish more clearly between the divinity and hypostatic identity of the Holy Spirit (which is a received dogma of our Churches) and the manner of the Spirit's origin, which still awaits full and final ecumenical resolution.</li>
<li>That those engaged in dialogue on this issue distinguish, as far as possible, the theological issues of the origin of the Holy Spirit from the ecclesiological issues of primacy and doctrinal authority in the Church, even as we pursue both questions seriously, together.</li>
<li>That the theological dialogue between our Churches also give careful consideration to the status of later councils held in both our Churches after those seven generally received as ecumenical.</li>
<li>That the Catholic Church, as a consequence of the normative and irrevocable dogmatic value of the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed#Niceno.E2.80.93Constantinopolitan_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Creed of 381</a>, use the original Greek text alone in making translations of that Creed for catechetical and liturgical use.</li>
<li>That the Catholic Church, following a growing theological consensus, and in particular the statements made by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_VI" title="Pope Paul VI">Pope Paul VI</a>, declare that the condemnation made at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Lyons" title="Second Council of Lyons">Second Council of Lyons</a> (1274) of those "who presume to deny that the Holy Spirit proceeds eternally from the Father and the Son" is no longer applicable.</li>
</ol>
<p>In the judgment of the consultation, the question of the <i>Filioque</i> is no longer a "Church-dividing" issue, which would impede full reconciliation and full communion. It is for the bishops of the Catholic and Orthodox Churches to review this work and to make whatever decisions would be appropriate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003_39-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003-39">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Summary">Summary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Summary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>While the Filioque doctrine was traditional in the West, being declared dogmatically in 447 by Pope Leo I, the Pope whose <i>Tome</i> was approved at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[234]</a></sup> its inclusion in the Creed appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Arian_controversy" title="Arian controversy">anti-Arian</a> situation of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Spain#Visigothic_Hispania_.285th.E2.80.938th_centuries.29" title="History of Spain">7th-century Spain</a>. However, this dogma was never accepted in the East. The <i>Filioque</i>, included in the Creed by certain anti-Arian councils in Spain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff199637_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff199637-259">[235]</a></sup> was a means to affirm the full divinity of the Son in relation to both the Father and the Spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIrvinSunquist2001340_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrvinSunquist2001340-92">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPapadakisMeyendorff1994228_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPapadakisMeyendorff1994228-261">[237]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ironically, a similar anti-Arian emphasis also strongly influenced the development of the liturgy in the East, for example, in promoting prayer to "Christ Our God", an expression which also came to find a place in the West,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[239]</a></sup> where, largely as a result of "the Church's reaction to Teutonic Arianism", "'Christ our God' ... gradually assumes precedence over 'Christ our brother'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[240]</a></sup> In this case, a common adversary, namely, <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arianism</a>, had profound, far-reaching effects, in the orthodox reaction in both East and West.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Stay_on_topic" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles"><span title="The paragraph near this tag may contain information that is not relevant to the article's main topic. (November 2015)">relevant?</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Filioque" title="Talk:Filioque">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Church politics, authority conflicts, ethnic hostility, linguistic misunderstanding, personal rivalry, forced conversions, large scale wars, political intrigue, unfilled promises and secular motives all combined in various ways to divide East and West.</p>
<p>The doctrine expressed by the phrase in Latin (in which the word "procedit" that is linked with "Filioque" does not have exactly the same meaning and overtones as the word used in Greek) is definitively upheld by the Western Church, having been dogmatically declared by Leo I,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCC247_51-4"><a href="#cite_note-CCC247-51">[50]</a></sup> and upheld by councils at Lyon and Florence<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones2006208.E2.80.93209_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones2006208.E2.80.93209-8">[8]</a></sup> that the Western Church recognizes as ecumenical, by the unanimous witness of the Latin Church Fathers (as Maximus the Confessor acknowledged) and even by Popes who, like Leo III, opposed insertion of the word into the Creed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulgakov200492_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulgakov200492-265">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECongar195953_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECongar195953-266">[242]</a></sup></p>
<p>That the doctrine is heretical is something that not all Orthodox now insist on. According to Ware, many Orthodox (whatever may be the doctrine and practice of the Eastern Orthodox Church itself) hold that, in broad outline, to say the Spirit proceeds from the Father <i>and</i> the Son amounts to the same thing as to say that the Spirit proceeds from the Father <i>through</i> the Son, a view accepted also by the Greeks who signed the act of union at the Council of Florence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare2006208_220-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare2006208-220">[199]</a></sup> For others, such as Bolotov and his disciples, the <i>Filioque</i> can be considered a Western <i>theologoumenon</i>, a theological opinion of <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a> that falls short of being a dogma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEECT2005.22Filioque.22_183-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEECT2005.22Filioque.22-183">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAOJDC1984n._45_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAOJDC1984n._45-221">[200]</a></sup> Bulgakov also stated: "There is no dogma of the relation of the Holy Spirit to the Son and therefore particular opinions on this subject are not heresies but merely dogmatic hypotheses, which have been transformed into heresies by the schismatic spirit that has established itself in the Church and that eagerly exploits all sorts of liturgical and even cultural differences."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulgakov2004148_222-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulgakov2004148-222">[201]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">
<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">
<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P_christianity.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="400" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/31px-P_christianity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/47px-P_christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/62px-P_christianity.svg.png 2x" width="31"/></a></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Christianity" title="Portal:Christianity">Christianity portal</a></span></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-Epiphanius-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Epiphanius_24-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The longer form of the creed of Epiphanius (374) included the doctrine: <span lang="grc">ἄκτιστον, ἐκ τοῦ πατρός ἐκπορευόμενον καί ἐκ τοῦ υίοῦ λαμβανόμενον</span> ("uncreated, who proceeds from the Father and is received from the Son").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-56">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFCongar1959">Congar (1959</a>, pp. 30–31) points out that provincialism – about theological terms which shape ideas in source languages but do not map to exact terms in target languages, including: <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration">prosōpon</span></i>, <i><span lang="la">hypostasis</span></i>, and <i><span lang="la">substantia</span></i> – contributes to "estrangement on the level of thought and mutual understanding."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ephesus I canon 7 was translated into English in the late 19th century in <a href="#CITEREFPercival1900">Percival (1900</a>, pp. 231–234) and translated in the late 20th century in <a href="#CITEREFTanner1990">Tanner (1990</a>, pp. <a class="external text" href="http://www.intratext.com/IXT/ENG0835/_P12.HTM" rel="nofollow">65–66</a>)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Indications of "filioque language can also be found in certain early Syriac sources," according to <a href="#CITEREFPlested2011">Plested (2011)</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">None of the creeds from the different stages in the Church's life can be considered superseded or irrelevant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[70]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An additional profession of faith in the acts of Toledo III, The Profession of Faith of King Reccaredus, included the doctrine but not the term: "<i><span lang="la">Spiritus aeque Sanctus confitendus a nobis et praedicandus est a Patre et Filio procedere et cum Patre et Filio unius esse substantiae</span></i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._470_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDH2012n._470-80">[75]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The Holy Ghost is from the Father and the Son, neither made, nor created, nor begotten, but proceeding". In the original Latin: "<span lang="la">Spiritus Sanctus a Patre et Filio: non factus, nec creatus, nec genitus, sed procedens</span>".</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-96"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-96">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While Reccared I converted to Catholicism, his successor <a href="/wiki/Liuva_II" title="Liuva II">Liuva II</a> reverted to Arianism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHinson1995220_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHinson1995220-94">[87]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-108"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-108">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBoulnois2003">Boulnois (2003</a>, pp. 106–107) notes that some ascribe an opinion about the <i>Filioque</i> to Cyril of Alexandria by "quotations grouped in anthologies" without analysis or context. The reason Cyril asserted a dependence was "the continuity between economy and theology" in his analysis of the relationship between the Son and the Holy Spirit. Cyril's reasons "correspond to different mechanisms" within the Trinity "which break up the simplistic opposition between the Latin schema of the triangle and the Greek model of the straight line." Boulnois thinks it is "impossible to classify Cyril unilaterally by applying [ ] a later conflict which, [ ] is largely alien to him."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-125"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-125">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Charlemagne's legates claimed that Tarasius, at his installation, did not follow the Nicene faith and profess that the Spirit proceeds from the Father and the Son, but confessed rather his procession from the Father <i>through the Son</i> (Mansi 13.760). The Pope strongly rejected Charlemagne's protest, showing at length that Tarasius and the Council, on this and other points, maintained the faith of the Fathers (ibid. 759–810).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Following this exchange of letters with the pope, Charlemagne commissioned the <i>Libri Carolini</i> (791–793) to challenge the positions both of the iconoclast council of 754 and of the Council of Nicaea of 787 on the veneration of icons. Again because of poor translations, the Carolingians misunderstood the actual decision of the latter Council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENAOCTC2003-39">[38]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-137"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-137">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Leo III defended the Filioque outside the Creed.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-176"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-176">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Similarly Moltmann observes that "the filioque was never directed against the 'monarchy' of the Father" and that the principle of the "monarchy" has "never been contested by the theologians of the Western Church." If these statements can be accepted by the Western theologians today in their full import of doing justice to the principle of the Father's "monarchy," which is so important to Eastern triadology, then the theological fears of Easterners about the filioque would seem to be fully relieved. Consequently, Eastern theologians could accept virtually any of the Memorandum's alternate formulae in the place of the filioque on the basis of the above positive evaluation of the filioque which is in harmony with Maximos the Confessor's interpretation of it. As Zizioulas incisively concludes: The "golden rule" must be Maximos the Confessor's explanation concerning Western pneumatology: by professing the filioque our Western brethren do not wish to introduce another <span lang="grc">αἴτον</span> in God's being except the Father, and a mediating role of the Son in the origination of the Spirit is not to be limited to the divine Economy, but relates also to the divine <span lang="grc">οὐσία</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStylianopoulous198429.E2.80.9330_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStylianopoulous198429.E2.80.9330-175">[163]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-191"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-191">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Pomazansky wrote that "Maximus the Confessor [...] justified [the Westerners] by saying that by the words 'from the Son' [the Westerners] intended to indicate that the Holy Spirit is <i>given</i> to creatures through the Son, that He is <i>manifested</i>, that He is <i>sent</i> — but not that the Holy Spirit has His existence from Him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPomazansky1984.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.intratext.com.2FIXT.2FENG0824.2F_P14.HTM_.22On_the_procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22.5D_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPomazansky1984.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.intratext.com.2FIXT.2FENG0824.2F_P14.HTM_.22On_the_procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22.5D-190">[177]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-193"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-193">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In icons<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Dating from when? (November 2015)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> of the Second Ecumenical Council, St. Gregory is presented as the recording clerk of the Synod, "and, as is believed, was the one who gave the final form to the Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed and formulated the article about the Holy Spirit: "And in the Holy Spirit, the Lord, the giver of life; Who proceedeth from the Father; Who with the Father and the Son is worshipped and glorified, Who spake by the Prophets".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[178]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-196"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-196">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the Byzantine period, the Orthodox side accused the Latin-speaking Christians, who supported the <i>Filioque</i>, of introducing two Gods, precisely because they believed that the <i>Filioque</i> implied two causes — not simply two sources or principles — in the Holy Trinity. The Greek <a href="/wiki/Patristics" title="Patristics">Patristic</a> tradition, at least since the <a href="/wiki/Cappadocian_Fathers" title="Cappadocian Fathers">Cappadocian Fathers</a> identified God with the person of the Father, whereas, St. Augustine seems to identify him with the one divine substance (the <i>deitas</i> or <i>divinitas</i>) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZizioulas1996_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZizioulas1996-195">[180]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-199"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-199">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Photius states in section 32 "And Again, if the Spirit proceeds from the Father, and the Son likewise is begotten of the Father, then it is in precisely this fact that the Father's personal property is discerned. But if the Son is begotten and the Spirit proceed from the Son (as this <a href="/wiki/Delirium" title="Delirium">delirium</a> of theirs would have it) then the Spirit of the Father is distinguished by more personal properties than the Son of the Father: on the one hand as proceeding from the equality of the Son and the Spirit, the Spirit is further differentiated by the two distinctions brought about by the dual procession, then the Spirit is not only differentiated by more distinctions than the Son of the Father, but the Son is closer to the Father's essence. And this is so precisely because the Spirit is distinguished by two specific properties. Therefore He is inferior to the Son, Who in turn is of the same nature as the Father! Thus the Spirit's equal dignity is blasphemed, once again giving rise to the Macedonian insanity against the Spirit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFarrell198775.E2.80.9376_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFarrell198775.E2.80.9376-189">[176]</a></sup> to the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Macedonius_I_of_Constantinople" title="Macedonius I of Constantinople">Macedonius I of Constantinople</a> and his sect called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pneumatomachians" title="Pneumatomachians">Pneumatomachians</a> in that the Holy Spirit is created by the Son and a servant of the Father and the Son. It was Macedonius' position that caused the specific wording of the section on the Holy Spirit by St <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Nyssa" title="Gregory of Nyssa">Gregory of Nyssa</a> in the finalized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicene_creed" title="Nicene creed">Nicene creed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPomazansky1984.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.intratext.com.2FIXT.2FENG0824.2F_P17.HTM_.22The_equality_of_honor_and_the_Divinity_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22.5D_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPomazansky1984.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.intratext.com.2FIXT.2FENG0824.2F_P17.HTM_.22The_equality_of_honor_and_the_Divinity_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22.5D-198">[182]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-200">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"However, the chief of the heretics who distorted the apostolic teaching concerning the Holy Spirit was" <a href="/wiki/Macedonius_I_of_Constantinople" title="Macedonius I of Constantinople">Macedonius I of Constantinople</a>, in the 4th century, who found followers "among former Arians and Semi-Arians. He called the Holy Spirit a creation of the Son, and a servant of the Father and the Son. Accusers of his heresy were" Church Fathers like <a href="/wiki/Basil_of_Caesarea" title="Basil of Caesarea">Basil of Caesarea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Nazianzus" title="Gregory of Nazianzus">Gregory of Nazianzus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Athanasius_of_Alexandria" title="Athanasius of Alexandria">Athanasius of Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Nyssa" title="Gregory of Nyssa">Gregory of Nyssa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ambrose" title="Ambrose">Ambrose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphilochius_of_Iconium" title="Amphilochius of Iconium">Amphilochius of Iconium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_of_Tarsus" title="Diodorus of Tarsus">Diodorus of Tarsus</a>, "and others, who wrote works against the heretics. The false teaching of Macedonius was refuted first in a series of local councils and finally at" Constantinople I. "In preserving Orthodoxy," Nicaea I completed the Nicaean Symbol of Faith "with these words: 'And in the Holy Spirit, the Lord, the Giver of Life, Who proceedeth from the Father, Who with the Father and the Son is equally worshiped and glorified, Who spake by the Prophets,' as well as those articles of the Creed which follow this in the Nicaean-Constantinopolitan Symbol of Faith."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPomazansky1984.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.intratext.com.2FIXT.2FENG0824.2F_P17.HTM_.22The_equality_of_honor_and_the_Divinity_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22.5D_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPomazansky1984.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.intratext.com.2FIXT.2FENG0824.2F_P17.HTM_.22The_equality_of_honor_and_the_Divinity_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22.5D-198">[182]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-206"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-206">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lossky wrote: "If the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Father alone, as the hypostatic cause of the consubstantial hypostases, we find the 'simple Trinity,' where the monarchy of the Father conditions the personal diversity of the Three while at the same time expressing their essential unity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELossky2003176_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELossky2003176-205">[187]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-208"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-208">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the Byzantine period the Orthodox side accused the Latin speaking Christians, who supported the <i>Filioque</i>, of introducing two Gods, precisely because they believed that the <i>Filioque</i> implied two causes—not simply two sources or principles—in the Holy Trinity. The Greek Patristic tradition, at least since the Cappadocian Fathers identified God with the person of the Father, whereas, Augustine seems to identify him with the one divine substance (the <i>deitas</i> or <i>divinitas</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZizioulas1996_195-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZizioulas1996-195">[180]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-210"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-210">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gregory Palamas asserted, in 1351, "that the Holy Spirit 'has the Father as foundation, source, and cause,' but 'reposes in the Son' and 'is sent – that is, manifested – through the Son.' (ibid. 194) In terms of the transcendent divine energy, although not in terms of substance or hypostatic being, 'the Spirit pours itself out from the Father through the Son, and, if you like, from the Son over all those worthy of it,' a communication which may even be broadly called 'procession' (ekporeusis)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[189]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Lossky-233"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Lossky_233-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lossky_233-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Lossky wrote that for Khomyakov, "legal formalism and logical rationalism of the Roman Catholic Church have their roots in the Roman State. These features developed in it more strongly than ever when the Western Church without consent of the Eastern introduced into the Nicean Creed the <i>filioque</i> clause. Such arbitrary change of the creed is an expression of pride and lack of love for one's brethren in the faith. 'In order not to be regarded as a schism by the Church, Romanism was forced to ascribe to the bishop of Rome absolute infallibility.' In this way Catholicism broke away from the Church as a whole and became an organization based upon external authority. Its unity is similar to the unity of the state: it is not super-rational but rationalistic and legally formal. Rationalism has led to the doctrine of the works of superarogation, established a balance of duties and merits between God and man, weighing in the scales sins and prayers, trespasses and deeds of expiation; it adopted the idea of transferring one person's debts or credits to another and legalized the exchange of assumed merits; in short, it introduced into the sanctuary of faith the mechanism of a banking house."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[211]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Stay_on_topic" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles"><span title="The material near this tag may contain information that is not relevant to the article's main topic. (November 2015)">relevant?</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Filioque#Khomyakov_in_Lossky" title="Talk:Filioque">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-241"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-241">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The <i>Filioque</i> does not concern the <span lang="grc">ἐκπόρευσις</span> of the Spirit issued from the Father as source of the Trinity," according to <a href="#CITEREFPCPCU1995">PCPCU (1995)</a>, "but manifests his <span lang="grc">προϊέναι</span> (<i><span lang="la">processio</span></i>) in the consubstantial communion of the Father and the Son, while excluding any possible subordinationist interpretation of the Father's monarchy".</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-249"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-249">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lossky wrote that "Whether we like it or not, the question of the procession of the Holy Spirit has been the sole dogmatic grounds for the separation of East and West. All the other divergences which, historically, accompanied or followed the first dogmatic controversy about the Filioque, in the measure in which they too had some dogmatic importance, are more or less dependent upon that original issue. [...] If other questions have arisen and taken the first place in more recent inter-confessional debates, that is chiefly because the dogmatic plane on which the thought of theologians operates is no longer the same as it was in the medieval period."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELossky2003163_187-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELossky2003163-187">[174]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Double_Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Double_Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22_10-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Double_Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22_10-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Double_Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22_10-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Double_Procession_of_the_Holy_Spirit.22_10-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFODCC2005">ODCC 2005</a>, "Double Procession of the Holy Spirit".</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201017-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201017_12-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFSiecienski2010">Siecienski 2010</a>, p. 17.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEK.C3.A4rkk.C3.A4inen2010276-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK.C3.A4rkk.C3.A4inen2010276_13-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFK.C3.A4rkk.C3.A4inen2010">Kärkkäinen 2010</a>, p. 276.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Basil of Caesarea <i>De Spiritu Sancto</i> 18.45 (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Nicene_and_Post-Nicene_Fathers:_Series_II/Volume_VIII/De_Spiritu_Sancto/Chapter_18" title="s:Nicene and Post-Nicene Fathers: Series II/Volume VIII/De Spiritu Sancto/Chapter 18">NPNF2 8:28</a>), in <a href="#CITEREFAnderson1980">Anderson (1980</a>, p. 72)</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Romanides1-134"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Romanides1_134-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Romanides1_134-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Romanides, John S. <a class="external text" href="http://www.romanity.org/htm/rom.03.en.franks_romans_feudalism_and_doctrine.03.htm" rel="nofollow">"Franks, Romans, feudalism, and doctrine"</a>. <i>romanity.org</i>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFilioque&amp;rft.atitle=Franks%2C+Romans%2C+feudalism%2C+and+doctrine&amp;rft.aufirst=John+S.&amp;rft.aulast=Romanides&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.romanity.org%2Fhtm%2From.03.en.franks_romans_feudalism_and_doctrine.03.htm&amp;rft.jtitle=romanity.org&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarnack1898.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fharnack.2Fdogma5.ii.ii.i.vi.iv.html_ch._6_.C2.A72.5D-139"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarnack1898.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fharnack.2Fdogma5.ii.ii.i.vi.iv.html_ch._6_.C2.A72.5D_139-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarnack1898.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fharnack.2Fdogma5.ii.ii.i.vi.iv.html_ch._6_.C2.A72.5D_139-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHarnack1898">Harnack 1898</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.ccel.org/ccel/harnack/dogma5.ii.ii.i.vi.iv.html" rel="nofollow">ch. 6 §2</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBray1983121-140"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBray1983121_140-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBray1983">Bray 1983</a>, p. 121.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaff1885.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fhcc4.i.v.iv.html_.C2.A770.5D-141"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaff1885.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.ccel.org.2Fccel.2Fschaff.2Fhcc4.i.v.iv.html_.C2.A770.5D_141-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFSchaff1885">Schaff 1885</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.ccel.org/ccel/schaff/hcc4.i.v.iv.html" rel="nofollow">§70</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Photius.22-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEODCC2005.22Photius.22_142-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFODCC2005">ODCC 2005</a>, "Photius".</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEChadwick2003154-143"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChadwick2003154_143-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFChadwick2003">Chadwick 2003</a>, p. 154: "Photius could concede that the Spirit proceeds through the Son in his temporal mission in the created order but not in his actual eternal being"</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff1986.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.myriobiblos.gr.2Ftexts.2Fenglish.2FMeyendorff_12.html_.C2.A72.5D-144"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff1986.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.myriobiblos.gr.2Ftexts.2Fenglish.2FMeyendorff_12.html_.C2.A72.5D_144-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMeyendorff1986">Meyendorff 1986</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.myriobiblos.gr/texts/english/Meyendorff_12.html" rel="nofollow">§2</a>: "<a href="/wiki/Nikephoros_Blemmydes" title="Nikephoros Blemmydes">Blemmydes</a> [... was] committed to [...] church unity and defended the idea that the image of the Spirit's procession 'through the Son', can serve as a bridge between the two theologies. [... He] collected patristic texts using the formula 'through the Son' and attacked those Greeks who out of anti-Latin zeal, were refusing to give it enough importance. In general, and already since Photius, the Greek position consisted in distinguishing the <i>eternal</i> procession of the Son from the Father, and the <i>sending</i> of the Spirit <i>in time</i> through the Son and by the Son. This distinction between the eternal processions and temporal manifestations was among the Byzantines the standard explanation for the numerous New Testament passages, where Christ is described as 'giving' and 'sending' the Spirit, and where the Spirit is spoken of as the 'Spirit of the Son'. In his letters [...] Blemmydes [...] avoided the distinction between eternity and time: the patristic formula 'through the Son' reflected both the eternal relationships of the divine Persons and the level of the 'economy' in time."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-145"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-145">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Photius, <i>Epistula 2</i> (PG 102:721–741).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEPapadakis1997113-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPapadakis1997113_146-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPapadakis1997113_146-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFPapadakis1997">Papadakis 1997</a>, p. 113.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTELossky2003168-147"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTELossky2003168_147-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTELossky2003168_147-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFLossky2003">Lossky 2003</a>, p. 168.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulgakov2004144-148"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulgakov2004144_148-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBulgakov2004">Bulgakov 2004</a>, p. 144.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulgakov200480-149"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulgakov200480_149-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBulgakov2004">Bulgakov 2004</a>, p. 80.</span></li>

Ὁ μὲν γὰρ ὁ θεὸς ήμάν φησί: "τὸ πνεῦμα τῆς ἀληθείας ὃ παρὰ τοῦ πατρὸς ἐκπορεύεται". Οἱ δὲ τῆς κοινῆς δυσσεβείας πατέρος τὸ πνεῦμα φασὶν, ὃ παρὰ τοῦ πατρός καὶ τοῦ υἱοῦ ἐκπορεύεται</span>"</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010163-171"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010163_171-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFSiecienski2010">Siecienski 2010</a>, p. 163: "This teaching neither denied the monarchy of the Father (who remained principal cause) nor did it imply two causes, since the Latins affirmed that the Son is, with the Father, a single spirating principle"</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski201081-172"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski201081_172-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFSiecienski2010">Siecienski 2010</a>, p. 81: "Maximus affirmed that the Latin teaching in no way violated the monarchy of the Father, who remained the sole cause (μία αἰτἰα) of both the Son and the Spirit"</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010127-173"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010127_173-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFSiecienski2010">Siecienski 2010</a>, p. 127: "In advocating the <i>filioque</i>, Bonaventure was careful to protect the monarchy of the Father, affirming that the 'Father is properly the One without an originator,... the Principle who proceeds from no other, the Father as such'"</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010105-174"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiecienski2010105_174-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFSiecienski2010">Siecienski 2010</a>, p. 105: "While clearly affirming the monarchy of the Father, who remained 'fountain and origin of the whole Trinity (<i>fons et origo totius Trinitatis</i>), so too is the Latin teaching"</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff1986.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.myriobiblos.gr.2Ftexts.2Fenglish.2FMeyendorff_13.html_.C2.A73.5D-216"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyendorff1986.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.myriobiblos.gr.2Ftexts.2Fenglish.2FMeyendorff_13.html_.C2.A73.5D_216-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMeyendorff1986">Meyendorff 1986</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.myriobiblos.gr/texts/english/Meyendorff_13.html" rel="nofollow">§3</a>: "The Orthodox side, [...] was gradually transcending a purely defensive stand, by discovering that the real problem of the <i>Filioque</i> lies not in the formula itself, but in the definition of God as <i>actus purus</i> as finalized in the <i>De ente et essentia</i> of Thomas Aquinas, vis-à-vis the more personalistic trinitarian vision inherited by the Byzantines from the Cappadocian Fathers."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-243"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-243">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFSiecienski2010">Siecienski 2010</a>, p. 90: "Adhering to the Eastern tradition, <a href="/wiki/John_of_Damascus" title="John of Damascus">John</a> affirmed (as Maximus had a century earlier) that 'the Father alone is cause [αἴτιος]' of both the Son and the Spirit, and thus "we do not say that the Son is a cause or a father, but we do say that He is from the Father and is the Son of the Father'."</span></li>

</ol>

In Roberts, Alexander; Donaldson, James; Coxe, A. Cleveland. <i>Latin Christianity: its founder, Tertullian</i>. The Ante-Nicene Fathers: the writings of the fathers down to A.D. 325. <b>3</b>. Chapter translated by Peter Holmes (American ed.). Buffalo: Christian Literature. <a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a>.</span><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.btitle=%5B%5BFile%3AWikisource-logo.svg%7C12px%7Calt%3DWikisource+link+to%5D%5D%26nbsp%3B%5B%5Bwikisource%3AAnte-Nicene+Fathers%2FVolume+III%2FAnti-Marcion%2FAgainst+Praxeas%7CAgainst+Praxeas%5D%5D&amp;rft.atitle=Latin+Christianity%3A+its+founder%2C+Tertullian&amp;rft.aulast=Tertullian&amp;rft.au=Tertullian&amp;rft.date=1887&amp;rft.series=The+Ante-Nicene+Fathers%3A+the+writings+of+the+fathers+down+to+A.D.+325&amp;rft.volume=3&amp;rft.edition=American&amp;rft.place=Buffalo&amp;rft.pub=Christian+Literature.+%5B%5BWikisource%5D%5D&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/en.wikipedia.org:Filioque"><span style="display: none;"> </span></span></li>

<li><cite class="citation book" id="CITEREFBauerschmidt2005">Thomas Aquinas (2005). <li><cite class="citation book" id="CITEREFWare1993">Ware, Timothy (later religious name Kallistos) (2007-05-29) [1993]. <a class="external text" href="http://www.intratext.com/x/eng0804.htm" rel="nofollow"><i>The Orthodox Church</i></a> (IntraText CT ed.). Rome: Èulogos SpA – via intratext.com.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFilioque&amp;rft.aufirst=Timothy+%28later+religious+name+Kallistos%29&amp;rft.aulast=Ware&amp;rft.btitle=The+Orthodox+Church&amp;rft.date=2007-05-29&amp;rft.edition=IntraText+CT&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.intratext.com%2Fx%2Feng0804.htm&amp;rft.place=Rome&amp;rft.pub=%C3%88ulogos+SpA&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
<li><cite class="citation book" id="CITEREFWill1861">Will, Cornelius, ed. (1861). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=97IWAAAAQAAJ&amp;pg=PA155" rel="nofollow">"Edictum Pseudosynodi Constantinopolitanae"</a>. <i>Acta et scripta quae de controversiis ecclesiae Graecae et Latinae saeculo undecimo composita extant</i> (in Greek and Latin). Paris [u.a.]: Lipsiae et Marpurgi. pp. 155–168. <a href="/wiki/OCLC" title="OCLC">OCLC</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/oclc/680484996" rel="nofollow">680484996</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFilioque&amp;rft.atitle=Edictum+Pseudosynodi+Constantinopolitanae&amp;rft.btitle=Acta+et+scripta+quae+de+controversiis+ecclesiae+Graecae+et+Latinae+saeculo+undecimo+composita+extant&amp;rft.date=1861&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D97IWAAAAQAAJ%26pg%3DPA155&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F680484996&amp;rft.pages=155-168&amp;rft.place=Paris+%5Bu.a.%5D&amp;rft.pub=Lipsiae+et+Marpurgi&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Filioque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Bradshaw, David. <b>Aristotle East and West: Metaphysics and the Division of Christendom</b>. Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2004, pp. 214–220.</li>
<li>Farrell, Joseph P. <b><a class="external text" href="http://dialectic.wordpress.com/ghd/" rel="nofollow">God, History, &amp; Dialectic: The Theological Foundations of the Two Europes and Their Cultural Consequences</a></b>. Bound edition 1997. Electronic edition 2008.</li>
<li>Groppe, Elizabeth Teresa. <b>Yves Congar's Theology of the Holy Spirit</b>. New York: Oxford University Press, 2004. See esp. pp. 75–79, for a summary of Congar's work on the <i>Filioque</i>. Congar is widely considered the most important Roman Catholic ecclesiologist of the twentieth century. He was influential in the composition of several Vatican II documents. Most important of all, he was instrumental in the association in the West of pneumatology and ecclesiology, a new development.</li>
<li>Haugh, Richard. <b>Photius and the Carolingians: The Trinitarian Controversy</b>. Belmont, MA: Nordland Publishing Company, 1975.</li>
<li>John St. H. Gibaut, "The <i>Cursus Honorum</i> and the Western Case Against Photius", <b>Logos</b> 37 (1996), 35–73.</li>
<li><cite class="citation book">Habets, Myk, ed. (2014). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=v9TeBAAAQBAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>Ecumenical perspectives on the Filioque for the 21st century</i></a>. T&amp;T Clark theology. London; New York: Bloomsbury Academic. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-56750072-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-56750072-4">978-0-56750072-4</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFilioque&amp;rft.btitle=Ecumenical+perspectives+on+the+Filioque+for+the+21st+century&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dv9TeBAAAQBAJ&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-56750072-4&amp;rft.place=London%3B+New+York&amp;rft.pub=Bloomsbury+Academic&amp;rft.series=T%26T+Clark+theology&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Jungmann" title="Joseph Jungmann">Jungmann, Joseph</a>. <b>Pastoral Liturgy</b>. London: Challoner, 1962. See "Christ our God", pp. 38–48.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/James_Likoudis" title="James Likoudis">Likoudis, James</a>. <b>Ending the Byzantine Greek Schism</b>. New Rochelle, New York: 1992. An apologetic response to polemical attacks. A useful book for its inclusion of important texts and documents; see especially citations and works by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, O.P., <a href="/wiki/Demetrios_Kydones" title="Demetrios Kydones">Demetrios Kydones</a>, Nikos A. Nissiotis, and Alexis Stawrowsky. The select bibliography is excellent. The author demonstrates that the <i>Filioque</i> dispute is only understood as part of a dispute over papal primacy and cannot be dealt with apart from <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiology" title="Ecclesiology">ecclesiology</a>.</li>
<li>Marshall, Bruce D. "'<b>Ex Occidente Lux?'</b> Aquinas and Eastern Orthodox Theology", <b>Modern Theology</b> 20:1 (January 2004), 23–50. Reconsideration of the views of Aquinas, especially on deification and grace, as well as his Orthodox critics. The author suggests that Aquinas may have a more accurate perspective than his critics, on the systematic questions of theology that relate to the <i>Filioque</i> dispute.</li>
<li>Reid, Duncan. <b>Energies of the Spirit: Trinitarian Models in Eastern Orthodox and Western Theology</b>. Atlanta, Georgia: Scholars Press, 1997.</li>
<li>Smith, Malon H. <b>And Taking Bread: Cerularius and the Azyme Controversy of 1054</b>. Paris: Beauschesne, 1978. This work is still valuable for understanding cultural and theological estrangement of East and West by the turn of the millennium. Now, it is evident that neither side understood the other; both Greek and Latin antagonists assumed their own practices were normative and authentic.</li>
<li>Webb, Eugene. <b>In Search of The Triune God: The Christian Paths of East and West</b>. Columbia, MO: University of Missouri Press, 2014.</li>
<li>Ware, Timothy (Kallistos). <b>The Orthodox Way</b>. Revised edition. Crestwood, New York: 1995, pp. 89–104.</li>
</ul>
